==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This thesis addresses the development of automated post­processing solutions for Vat
Photopolymerization (VPP) additive manufacturing (AM) farms.
The primary focus is
on overcoming the challenges associated with automating the hazardous steps in post­
processing, where uncured resin is present on the finished parts, in an effort to enhance
safety and reduce operator risk. The research covers the design and implementation of
automated systems for cleaning, aiming to enhance efficiency, reduce manual labour, and
enabling a VPP AM farm capable of running continuously.
The research includes a thorough analysis of existing post­processing methods in VPP,
identifying key safety concerns and limitations in the current manual methods. It then
details the creation of a universal mounting interface to simplify part handling through the
use of a collaborative robot (cobot). The development of modules engineered to operate
within an integrated system are presented. Innovative solvent­free resin removal and
collection within the modules is shown to reduce cost, resin use, and solvent use in an
AM farm.
Experimental studies validate the effectiveness of the proposed solutions, demonstrating
significant improvements in processing time and operator safety. The integration of these
automated systems controlled by a centralised controller further underscores the potential
for scalability in AM farms.
This work contributes to the advancement of AM technologies by providing practical solu­
tions for the automation of post­processing, thereby facilitating more efficient, safer, and
sustainable production practices in the field of VPP AM.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms

Acknowledgements
We want to express our gratitude and appreciation to the supervising team, Marina Arte­
meva, Christian Leslie Budden, Magnus Bolt Kjer, David Bue Pedersen, and Venkata
Karthik Nadimpalli. It has been a pleasure to learn from you all. Thank you for supporting
our, at times, crazy ideas and allowing us to create something we can be proud of.
We thank Aakil Raj Lalwani for always being there and Javier Lopez Navas for letting us
use all of the IPA, resin and gloves in the VPP lab.
Thank you to Jan Frank Pedersen and the workshop team for providing feedback and
producing high quality parts for our machines.
We would also like to extend our gratitude towards the industry partners Carmo A/S and
Krebs & Co A/S, where Alberto Basso, Lasse Thygesen, and Tobias Kristensen have
shown great support and provided guidance to the project.
Thank you to Jonas Lindegaard Mikkelsen for feedback and good advice.
We want to thank our partners for supporting us, even when we are spending all summer
at DTU.
Finally, we want to thank our families for always supporting and believing in us.

1
Introduction
In recent years, additive manufacturing (AM) technologies have gained significant popu­
larity due to increasing capabilities and decreasing costs. The expiration of patents and
proprietary technologies has made AM more accessible, benefiting a broad spectrum
of users, including advanced research and development facilities, as well as individual
hobbyists engaged in personal projects. There are several different AM technologies with
each their own set of strengths and weaknesses depending on the task and in this project,
bottom­up vat photopolymerization (VPP) is exclusively considered. The working princi­
ple of VPP involves a vat (container) of liquid photopolymer resin, which is exposed to a
light source such as a laser, commonly referred to as stereolithography (SLA), or other
methods such as digital light processing (DLP) and liquid crystal display mask projection
(LCD). The light source cures (solidifies) the resin in specific areas, forming a solid layer.
This process is repeated layer by layer until the entire object is built [1]. Figure 1.1a il­
lustrates the working principles of DLP and LCD and figure 1.1b illustrates a typical VPP
printed part; notice how the part is covered in residual liquid resin sticking to the surface.
VPP printed parts require post­processing before it is considered a finished part. The first
step in post­processing is to remove the excess liquid resin from the printed components.
It is important that all residues of the liquid resin are removed in order to obtain a clean sur­
face, to ensure the dimensional accuracy of the final product. Additionally, VPP printing
requires support structures to stabilise overhangs on the part. Support structures must be
removed before the part can be shipped. The removal of support structures and excess
resin is followed by post­curing of the component. This is done either by UV irradiation or
by heat treatment. Post­curing further cures the material enabling the parts to obtain full
strength.
The chemical reaction which enables the photopolymerization/curing of the resin, can be
seen in figure 1.2. It involves liquid resin, consisting of a mix of photo­initiators, photo­
inhibitor, crosslinkers, monomers and oligomers, being excited by UV­light initiating an
increase in viscosity and ultimately hardening of the constituents, forming a thermoset
polymer part [4].
VPP can be used to generate high­fidelity parts. A consumer printer, Sonic Mighty Revo,
has a printing XY­resolution of 16.8 µm x 24.8 µm and layer­height of 10 µm [6]. This
stands in stark contrast to the resolution achievable in fused deposition modelling (FDM):
XY­resolution: 200µm x 200µm and layer­height of 100 µm [7].
A pivotal moment occurred in 2016 when key patents related to VPP expired , allowing
other businesses to enter the market and produce lower­cost VPP machines [8]. This
development has enabled a broader range of users to create high­fidelity components at
a lower entry cost, supporting a wide variety of applications. This reduction in the cost
of resin 3D printers presents a significant business opportunity for the development of
AM farms that utilize VPP printing technologies. Smaller­scale production series of poly­
mer components could become more affordable and sustainable compared to traditional
injection moulding (IM) methods. However, large­scale VPP manufacturing presents nu­
merous challenges, particularly in the post­processing phase.
1.1
AM Farm Research Project
The AM Farm research project, which forms the basis of this thesis, aims to establish
the first Danish photopolymerization­based AM Farm. This initiative focuses on agile,
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
1
==PAGE:14==
(a) DLP and LCD VPP ­ From [2]
(b) Real life example of VPP ­ From [3]
Figure 1.1: VPP technologies in scope for this projects post­processing solutions
intermediate­sized production of 1,000 to 100,000 components, surpassing (IM) in both
agility and energy efficiency. The industrial partners Carmo A/S and J. KREBS & CO.
A/S aim to utilize the AM Farm, to offer intermediate­sized production volumes more eco­
nomically viable than IM, via a next­generation manufacturing facility [9, 10]. The AM
Farm project plan, suggest clustering into kernels, within the farm, divided by material
type and resin printer system, which enables rolling hardware upgrades to be executed.
The details, scope and goals of the AM Farm research project can be found in Appendix
A.4.3.
1.2
AM Farm Architecture
Developing a complete manufacturing system capable of automating the VPP process
chain is a complex task, where the functions and interfaces on each subsystem can affect
the integration and efficiency of subsequent modules in the manufacturing system [11].
The automated AM Farm is configured to streamline and optimise the production of com­
ponents with VPP. A proposal for a single kernel and its post­processing modules can be
seen in Figure 1.3. It is equipped with the necessary elements to support an automated
VPP production operation. In order to support the intermediate­sized production up to
100,000 components, multiple kernels will need to be installed in an AM farm. Develop­
ing the fully functioning AM farm production setup requires significant work beyond this
thesis, which is supported by Innovation Fund Denmark with 10.3 million DKK [12].
2
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:15==
Figure 1.2: Photopolymerisation process ­ From Marcarena et. al.[5]
Figure 1.3: System definition of an AM farm kernel. Adapted from Appendix A.4.3
The system integrates multiple modules to automate the entire post­processing workflow,
from part cleaning to final inspection and packaging of printed parts. The main compo­
nents and their primary function of the system include:
• VPP Printer kernel: A group of VPP printers responsible for producing the printed
parts.
• Automated Handling Unit: A robotic arm that manages the transfer of build plates
and printed parts between the modules.
• Cleaning Module: Responsible for cleaning the printed part for removing uncured
resin.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
3
==PAGE:16==
• Drying Module: Ensures parts are dry and free of residual solvents.
• Post­curing Module: Uses UV and/or thermal curing to develop the final mechan­
ical properties of the parts.
• Build Plate Removal Module: Automates the detachment of printed parts from the
build plate.
• Support Removal Module: Facilitates the removal of support structures from the
parts.
• Inspection and Packaging Module: Conducts quality control checks and pack­
ages the finished parts.
• Manufacturing Execution System (MES): Centralized system for monitoring and
controlling the entire workflow.
Each of the modules presented tackles a specific challenge in the process chain of VPP
parts production, which are currently performed manually in most cases. To make the
manufacturing facility a viable alternative to traditional IM plants, many automation solu­
tions will require the development of custom hardware.
The printer kernel, the automated handling unit, and the post­processing modules are
tied together and managed by the MES. The automated handling is proposed to be a
collaborative robot (cobot), which ensures a safe environment where human operators
can work alongside safely and efficiently [13].
The first step and a primary challenge in the VPP post­processing lies in cleaning off
uncured liquid resin from the printed parts. It involves interaction with hazardous uncured
resins and solvents and tedious manual labour and human operators needs personal
protection equipment (PPE). Further more, manual labour introduces variances in the
production chain and added running cost.
The following thesis will cover the post­processing chain of VVP prints in an AM farm scale,
particularly focusing on the development, design, and testing of automated solutions for
the cleaning of VPP printed parts. This is exclusively within the Cleaning Module which
can be seen in Figure 1.3. The research output aims to serve as an integral part of an
AM farm, where efficiency and safety are major concerns.
1.3
Thesis Scope
The scope of this thesis encompasses improving health and safety for the AM Farm,
automating the ”dirty” post­processing steps and ensuring economic and environmental
sustainable operations.
1.3.1
Health and Safety
A primary challenge in VPP post­processing lies in cleaning of uncured liquid resin from
the printed parts, which involves interaction with hazardous uncured resins and solvents.
Photopolymer resins often contain various acrylates and epoxies, which are known im­
mune sensitizers [14]. The acrylates and epoxies gives the resin the properties needed
for VPP, but it also poses a safety risk. Human contact with acrylates has been shown to
cause contact dermatitis [15]. The safety datasheet provided in appendix A.4.3 shows the
warnings related to the FTD Industrial Blend resin used throughout this project. Resins
and the solvents needed to clean the VPP parts are known for releasing volatile or­
ganic compounds, hazardous fumes, particles, and gases during the cleaning step [16,
4
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:17==
17].These substances are integral to the cleaning process, but pose serious risks to hu­
man health and the environment. For that reason, it is crucial to take the proper precau­
tions when working with resins. This includes personal protective equipment (PPE) and
proper training in handling these chemicals. Automation of cleaning tasks can mitigate
the exposure potential from manually handling VPP cleaning.
1.3.2
Automation
When scaling up the AM farm to be capable of producing intermediate­sized production
volumes via continuous printing with multiple VPP printers, automation is essential for
economic viability. Carmo A/S, an industry partner on the project, has stated a goal of
10 VPP printers per kernel, printing continuously with an average print time of 2 hours.
Printing continuously requires multiple build plates as a fresh build plate is needed to
immediately start a new print. The build plates must be leveled to the specific printer using
them to ensure the best quality of the prints.
A print time of 2 hours is considered fast
for small to medium sized prints for the current state of resin printing [2]. By starting each
print in succession and leveraging the full two hour print cycle, a takt time of 12 minutes
can be expected as shown in equation 1.1. This takt time is therefore the maximum time
allowed for each individual post­process step, before the process must be ready to receive
the next build plate.
Average time between finished prints = Time per print
Printers
= 120 min
10
= 12 min
(1.1)
This time of 12 minutes resembles a takt time for a production, where one build plate is
fully post­processed every 12 minutes.
Takt time is defined as the rate at which a finished product needs to be completed to meet
customer demand. In general terms, takt time can be calculated as:
Takt time = Time available
Demand
= Ta
D
(1.2)
In this scenario, the average time between finished prints (12 minutes) can be considered
similarly to the takt time, where the ”Time available” is equivalent to the total time for
one print cycle (120 minutes) and the ”Demand” is the number of prints produced by the
printers (10 prints). Thus,
Takt time = Ta
D = 120 min
10
= 12 min
(1.3)
Therefore, in this context, the average time between finished prints and the takt time are
equivalent, both being 12 minutes. Having a post­processing system capable of handling
this takt time is therefore considered a goal for this project.
The required manual post­processing would fully occupy an employee, who would then
be exposed to dangerous resin during the entire shift. It has been shown that automation
can improve both efficiency and reduce risks for the workers [18]. The overview of the
AM Farm kernel presented in Figure 1.3 shows a cobot at the center of a kernel. This
robot is an essential part of automating the post­processing stages, as it can interact with
contaminated surfaces and run continuously. A Fanuc CRX­10iA cobot was ordered at
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
5
==PAGE:18==
the beginning of this project, but it has not been delivered yet [19]. Having fully auto­
mated post­processing capabilities enables lights out operation (meaning fully automated
production requiring no human operators on­site) as discussed in Appendix A.4.3.
1.3.3
Sustainability
A large incentive for introducing AM farms using VPP was to improve production efficiency
and implement a sustainable approach to low to intermediate­sized production volumes,
compared to traditional IM. IM becomes more economically sustainable at large produc­
tion volumes, but the initial costs related to mould production and setup require significant
investments. This makes an AM VPP Farm attractive for low to medium sized production
volumes, where a lower CO2­e per part for VPP contributes to a total CO2­e reduction
compared to IM [20]. The largest environmental impact, when scaling up VPP manufac­
turing, comes from the waste stream generated from solvents saturated with resins from
the cleaning process. Incineration of these solvents, along with the production of new
solvents and resins, is a CO2 and energy­intensive process (from AM Farm Research
Project, Appendix A.4.3). This not only leads to significant greenhouse gas emissions but
also consumes considerable energy resources, thereby reducing the overall sustainabil­
ity benefits of using VPP. Implementing better waste management strategies and more
efficient solvent recycling processes could mitigate some of these environmental impacts.
1.4
Research Questions
The first steps in achieving full automation for an AM farm, involves executing hazardous
operations before the parts are cured. These operations, along with the machines used,
are referred to as minimum viable products (MVPs). The project aims at developing and
testing these MVPs to support the AM Farm, with the scope presented above. The scope
and purpose of this thesis leads to the following research questions:
• How can the hazardous post­processing steps in VPP be automated, to min­
imise risk to human health?
• Can the cleaning process be automated to ensure that parts are as clean as
those cleaned manually?
• What measures can be implemented in the post­processing chain to ensure
an economically and environmentally sustainable AM Farm?

2
State of the Art in VPP Post­Processing
Additive manufacturing, particularly VPP, has revolutionized the production of intricate
and high resolution components, offering significant advantages in precision and material
utilization [21]. However, the true potential of VPP can only be realised through effective
post­processing techniques and operational settings, which are critical for scaling this
technology for use in AM Farms. This chapter provides an overview of the current state­
of­the­art in post­processing and operational conditions that can enhance the efficacy of
VPP AM Farms. The subsequent sections will cover the following critical aspects in the
post­processing chain and showcase existing solutions. These solutions are primarily
industrial as the academic field within VPP post­processing is limited at this time.
• Build plate handling: Efficient handling techniques for build plates, including auto­
mated and manual systems to maintain throughput and quality in AM farms.
• Cleaning processes: State­of­the­art cleaning methodologies, such as agitation,
sonication cleaning, and chemical solvents used in the cleaning step.
• Part removal from build plate : Methods for removing printed parts form the build
plate, automated and manual solutions.
• Support removal: Technologies for support detachment and best practices for
manual support removal to enhance part aesthetics and functionality.
• Drying: Conventional methods used for drying the printed parts after cleaning with
solvent.
• Post­curing: UV and thermal curing processes, discussing their effectiveness in
enhancing part durability.
• AM Farm settings: Essential operational settings for scaling VPP in AM farms,
including workflow automation, printer integration, and maintenance strategies.
8
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:21==
2.1
Build Plate Mounting
The very first step needed in the AM farm, after a VPP print is finished, is dismounting
the build plate. This task should be performed by a cobot, to automate the workflow
throughout the process chain. Until recently, resin printer manufacturers primarily used a
screw­tightened pressure plate connection (Figure 2.1). In this setup, the build plate slides
onto the printer’s mounting arm and is secured with a large screw, ensuring a stable con­
nection during printing. Notable manufacturers employing this method include Creality,
Elegoo, Peopoly, and Anycubic.
Figure 2.1: Build plate mounting on the Elegoo Saturn printer ­ From [22]
High­end resin printers and newer consumer­grade resin printers have implemented other
types of mounting mechanisms, such as cam lock mechanisms, which simplify the user in­
teraction and allow quicker mounting and dismounting of build plates, the so­called ’quick­
release design’, which can be seen in Figure 2.2 [23]. Formlabs resin printers have had
this cam lock in their build plate mount since the first model [24].
Figure 2.2: Elegoo Saturn 4 Ultra ’Quick­release Design’ ­ From [23]
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
9
==PAGE:22==
Other manufacturers of more expensive resin printers, such as SprintRay [25], which
focuses mainly on the dental industry, have implemented a magnetic mounting solution,
where the printer arm houses an on­off magnet and a lever that switches the magnet on
and off for a secure and tight connection [26], which can be seen in Figure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Build Plate mounting arm on the SprintRay Pro S Dental 3D Printer ­ From
[25]
The traditional screw­tightened pressure plate, the cam lock mechanism, and similar con­
figurations manufactured by the aforementioned VPP machine manufacturers are all two­
step solutions using the following sequence:
1. Attach build plate to Z­stage arm
2. Lock free degrees of freedom (DoF) with screw or latch
Having multiple steps increases the complexity of implementing an automated robotic
build plate handling system. No current build plate mounting solution for consumer grade
printers, seem to be optimal for fully automating the workflow in an AM farm setting.
It is important that all DoFs are constrained and position is precisely defined when the
build plate is mounted. The build plate must be leveled and parallel in relation to the vat
to ensure successful print outcomes with the high resolution that VPP is known for. Fur­
thermore, if the printed parts are kept on the build plate during the subsequent steps in the
post­processing chain, the mounting mechanism needs to be versatile. The kinematics
of these solutions is highly dependent on the specific interfaces of the two components
(build plate and printer arm), so further investigation of the different manufacturers’ build
plate mounting solutions would be necessary to develop a ”all fitting” solution.
Automation of the build plate (dis)mounting and handling the mounting interface for attach­
ing build plates needs to be engineered for automation and robotics, hence a standard
screw­tightened connection is not applicable in an AM farm setting.
Artemeva et al. proposed an automatic coupling solution for build plate handling, specifi­
cally for AM farms [27]. This design proposal involves a precisely manufactured connector
interface for micrometer­level accuracy during docking. It implements a dual dovetail slid­
ing joint for constraining the build plate, and a solenoid actuator engaging two locking
balls for securing the build plate in all six DoF. With this solution, it will be necessary to in­
tegrate the solenoid actuator into the vertical axis of the selected resin printer. In addition,
a specialised gripper is proposed for a robotic arm to handle the transportation of build
plates in the AM Farm. The design is promising, as it ensures high precision and stability
10
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:23==
for the automated handling of build plates, which is critical to maintaining the quality and
efficiency of AM processes.
2.2
Cleaning
Cleaning involves removing the leftover uncured photopolymer that coats the part after
each finished print job. The cleaning step in the VPP process chain is known as the
most demanding and hazardous step. Commercial cleaning solutions for VPP are widely
available for both, hobbyists and full­scale mass production. Cleaning is typically done by
washing the part in a solvent.
2.2.1
Agitation Cleaning
The existing methods for agitation and cleaning use one of the following in combination
with a solvent:
• Stirring
Mechanical agitation of the liquid using an impeller or similar often mounted centrally
at the bottom of the cleaning chamber. The part is fully submerged in the solvent.
This method can be seen done both, with parts on the build plate, as well parts
removed from the build plate [28].
• Swirling
Rotation of the build plate in the solvent. The part is fully submerged in the solvent.
Similar to stirring, but one advantage is that spinning the build plate at the top of the
solvent limits the agitation of unwanted particles and residue, in the bottom of the
cleaning vat, from previously washed parts in the solvent.
• Jetting
The part is not submerged but is sprayed continuously with solvent. The solvent
is either circulated or pumped away. This method requires a robust pump system
and a fully enclosed container to avoid getting solvent on the surroundings. The
jetting method thoroughly washes the model by throwing the solvent at high speed,
allowing the fresh solvent to continuously reach the part and remove residual resin
[29].
• Soaking
Another simpler approach is soaking the part, with no agitation of the liquid. This
method solely relies on the diffusion and solubility of the resin in order to remove the
uncured resin. Soaking is typically done as a second cleaning step after cleaning
with a solvent and agitation [30].
2.2.2
Ultrasonic Cleaning
Another type of mechanical agitation, is ultrasonication. It is used in combination with a
solvent. In an ultrasonic cleaner, transducers attached to the bottom or sides of cleaning
tanks generate high­frequency sound waves. This technique generates cavitation bub­
bles in a cleaning liquid. The bubbles grow during the low­pressure cycle and collapse
during the high­pressure cycle, releasing energy in the form of shock waves, which gen­
erates intense scrubbing actions that clean surfaces efficiently [31], the process of bubble
formation and implosion is illustrated in Figure 2.4.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
11
==PAGE:24==
Figure 2.4: Bubble formation in during ultrasonication of solvents ­ From [32]
Cavitation improves cleaning efficiency by creating microscopic vapor­filled bubbles that,
upon impacting the sample’s surface and imploding, generate sufficient energy to dis­
lodge impurities. Nevertheless, while increased cavitation improves cleaning outcomes,
it carries the risk of permanently damaging the objects’ surface. Hence, it is important to
establish a cleaning protocol with carefully selected parameters like frequency and tem­
perature to prevent surface damage. The temperature can be managed based on the
ultrasonic device used. Ultrasonic waves increase the media temperature due to energy
deposition.[33]
Ultrasonic frequencies have a significant impact on the cleaning effect due to the be­
haviour of cavitation bubbles. The frequencies used in ultrasonic cleaning equipment
vary from 20 kHz up to 80 kHz. Low frequencies yield a longer bubble growth period,
resulting in larger bubbles and more powerful implosions. These intense implosions are
effective at removing large contaminants from surfaces. [32]
On the other hand, higher frequencies produce smaller bubbles with shorter growth pe­
riods. Although these bubbles have less powerful implosions, they can reach and clean
fine features and intricate surfaces more effectively. This makes high­frequency ultra­
sonication particularly suitable for cleaning delicate items or components with complex
geometries. The cleaning effect is thus a balancing act between the aggressiveness of
low frequency and the precision of high frequency, depending on the application require­
ments.[34]
2.2.3
Solvents for Cleaning
Solvents effectively clean resin parts from excess liquid resin via their ability to dissolve
the uncured resin material. This process is driven by the solvent molecules interacting
with the resin molecules, disrupting the cohesive forces that hold the resin together. The
solvent must be chemically suitable to effectively dissolve the resin, but also safe for the
integrity of the printed part [35].
The cleaning process is critically dependent on the solvent and its efficiency in dissolving
liquid resin. Various solvents are employed for this purpose, each with distinct chemical
properties and effectiveness. Understanding these solvents, ensures optimal cleaning.
Some of the commonly used solvents for cleaning are discussed below.
• Isopropyl Alcohol (IPA)
The most widely utilised solvent in the VPP post­processing industry is IPA. Its pop­
ularity stems from its availability, relatively low cost, and compatibility with most
resins. IPA must be stored away from ignition sources and properly handled in
compliance with ATEX regulations [36]. It is a highly volatile solvent, with a flash
point at 12 ◦C [37].
12
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:25==
• Glycol Ethers
Glycol ethers are a group of solvents known for their effectiveness in resin dissolu­
tion [38]. These solvents are generally less volatile and have higher flash and boiling
points, which makes them safer to use than IPA. It is effective at removing uncured
resin. Key examples of glycol ethers used in cleaning of resin parts include:
– Tripropylene Glycol Monomethyl Ether (TPM) TPM is a common solvent
in resin cleaning due to its high dissolving ability and low volatility. TPM can
dissolve roughly three times the amount of resin as IPA before losing its efficacy
[39]. It has a flash point at 124 ◦C making it substantially safer in a production
environment and thereby avoiding the need for ATEX directive requirements
due to the low temperature classification [36, 40].
– Diethylene Glycol Monobutyl Ether (DGBE) DGBE, often marketed as a
SLA cleaner, provides excellent cleaning performance with lower health risks
and reduced flammability, with a flash point at 105 °C [41]. It is known for
cleaning rapidly and can dissolve more resin than TPM before losing efficacy
[42]. Products such as those from Anamsi A/S and others utilize this solvent as
a primary active ingredient for its superior cleaning properties [43, 44, 42]. Re­
search from Danielak et al [45] indicated good cleaning results at a concentra­
tion of 50%/50% in volume (Anmasi/deionized water), practically no corrosion
to fine 3D printed details, compared to rather harsh corrosion from IPA. DGBE
is a safe solvent, that is approved for skin contact and is commonly present in
hair products and cosmetics [46].
Figure 2.5 illustrates the weight % of resin versus the time it takes to clean a 3D
printed part. The top performer, PLM­403­SUB, consists of 50% DGBE and the
remaining chemical is 1­Tetradecene which has an inhibiting effect on the polymer­
ization of monomers. Anmasi consists of at least 90% of DGBE with the remainder
being water [43, 41, 47].
Many resin­cleaning solvents originating from the group of glycol ethers are avail­
able on the market [44, 48, 49, 50]. These solvents offer low volatility and enhanced
safety profiles compared to IPA. Each solvent is engineered with specific chemical
properties that optimize its ability to dissolve resin effectively. Glycol ethers are par­
ticularly valued in industrial applications due to their efficacy and reduced safety and
health risks, making them a favored choice for resin cleaning [51].
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
13
==PAGE:26==
Figure 2.5: Saturation of various chemical solvents for resin removal from PostProcess
Technologies Inc. [42]. PLM­403­SUB consists of 50% DGBE, Anmasi in the undiluted
state contains at least 90% [47].
The efficiency of various cleaning methods, when used with the three different types of
solvents presented here, has been evaluated in an experimental study, detailed in Section
7.2.2.
2.2.4
DTU’s VPP Lab Standard Cleaning Procedure
A standard cleaning procedure has been established at the DTU VPP lab, which is in­
tended to yield safe and clean parts. It is informally formulated by trial and error and is
more a matter of craftsmanship than rigid science. It involves the following steps:
1. A light hand wash, where the part is manually brushed and sprayed with IPA in
combination with pressurized air, the process is repeated several times until there
are no visible residue of the uncured resin.
2. Mechanical agitation using a machine:
• Small parts (<10mm x 10mm): An ultrasonic bath where the parts re sub­
merged in a glass beaker containing IPA and ultrasonicated for 5 min at 80kHz
and 50% power and 25 °C.
• Large parts (>10mm x 10 mm): Submerging the parts in a Form Wash (mag­
netic stirrer) for a thorough cleaning, in IPA for 10 min.
3. Drying the part to remove residual solvent from the surface of the part with com­
pressed air.
4. If the part is not sufficiently rinsed, repeat the process and inspect between each
step.
Figure 2.6 illustrates each step in the protocol. After these steps, the part is subjectively
14
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:27==
inspected for cleanliness. If it is not satisfactorily clean, the operator will repeat the pro­
cedure until a satisfactory result is achieved.
Figure 2.6: Standard procedure from the DTU VPP lab
It should be noted that a combination of the magnetic stirrer and ultrasonication cleaning
are also employed for especially difficult parts with narrow deep blind holes and small
features. This combination of magnetic stirring followed by ultrasonication will be refereed
to as the ”standard procedure” for the rest of the project due to the nature of the benchmark
parts being used in the project.
2.2.5
Industry Cleaning Solutions
Formlabs’ Form Wash (Figure 2.7) has become the industry standard in many VPP labs
and over the years, it has proven to be a highly reliable machine. It features mechanical
agitation through a magnetic impeller at the bottom of the container. It includes useful
automation features for operators, such as automatic part retrieval and lids to limit IPA
evaporation, enhancing ease of use and the efficiency of the manual post­processing.
Most consumer­grade resin printer manufacturers sell a similar washing unit as the Form
Wash but in a simpler variant with fewer features, that essentially performs the same
cleaning procedure [28, 53]. SprintRay, the dental resin printer manufacturer, has devel­
oped a jetting cleaning system, with a two­stage cleaning using IPA, a coarse wash, and
subsequently a final wash. It is a fairly automatic solution that performs the two­stage
wash and drying of the part in just 9 minutes [54]. Most washing units directly intended
for post­processing resin­printed parts do not include ultrasonication but rely on the other
methods of agitating the solvent in a chamber. Specialised vendors such as Elma Ul­
trasonics, sell ultrasonic cleaners for general­purpose ultrasonic cleaning of components
[55].
PostProcess Technologies Inc.
specialises in developing automated solutions for the
post­printing phase of additive manufacturing on an industrial scale. They offer industrial­
sized machines with solvent capacities in the range of 150 liters up to 1000 liters [56]. The
resin cleaner machines from PostProcess use a specially developed patented cleaning
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
15
==PAGE:28==
Figure 2.7: Form Wash (2nd Generation) ­ From [52]
technology called Submersed Vortex Cavitation (SVC), illustrated in Figure 2.8a. SVC
technology utilizes a strategic pumping scheme that creates a rotating motion of the part
while submerged in a solvent. In addition to rotating the part in the solvent, the SVC
technology employs variable ultrasonics for thorough cleaning, proving effective for fine
features [57].
(a) PostProcess Technologies patented Submerged Vortex Cavitation method
­ From [57].
(b) Centrifuge for spinning of VPP parts developed by Carbon3D ­ From [58].
Figure 2.8: Industrial cleaning machines
16
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:29==
Carbon3D, the company that invented Continuous Liquid Interface Production technology
(CLIP) technology, which enables layerless high­speed resin printing [59], has developed
a cleaning solution that does not use solvents at all. They are instead using a centrifuge to
remove and reuse the excess resin from the build plates. The centrifuge was specifically
developed to assist in producing dental moulds for clear­aligner manufacturing at Derby’s
Dental [60]. The prints that are post­processed by this machine, are printed directly on the
build plate without supports and with little to no negative draft angles. This enables the
Carbon3D centrifuge to clean the parts enough that no solvent is needed for this process
and the parts are ready for curing directly after this spinning. This centrifuge is designed
for large build plates that are manually loaded and the design can be seen in Figure 2.8.
Carbon3D refers to the generation of hazardous waste as the ’hidden problem’ of AM,
particularly for liquid resin­based technology like VPP [58]. Recovering and reusing resin
that was otherwise considered waste is an addition to the post­processing steps that few
other companies have implemented.
2.3
Part Removal from Build Plate
The printed parts adheres to the build plate through the first few burn­in layers with a
longer curing time to ensure attachment. Currently, the standard method for removing
printed parts from the build plate is completely manual. The part is usually detached
using a spatula, where the operator needs to be careful not to break the part. Due to
the strong adhesion of the part to the build plate, significant force is required to detach it.
Many consumer­grade printers have the possibility to add flexible magnetic build plates
for easy and fast part removal simply by flexing the build plate to pop parts off the build
surface [61].
Figure 2.9: Standard manual method for removing parts from build plate ­ From [62]
Other methods of scaling up manufacturing capabilities with the use of automation are
continuous printing techniques. HeyGears, Formlabs, and more have developed methods
for removing the part from the build plate directly after printing and ejecting them in a UV­
protective bin for later post­processing [63, 64, 65]. That way, the post­processing can
be handled in bulk, and the printer can continue with another print without the need for
an operator to post­process the print before another can be started. HeyGears’ solution
called HIVE has implemented a scraping knife that cuts the parts of the build plate [64].
The Formlabs Form Auto, seen in Figure 2.10 has implemented a flexible build plate
that flexes the parts off of the build plate before it continues printing. Both are extension
modules for their VPP systems.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
17
==PAGE:30==
Figure 2.10: Formlabs Form Auto unit for autonomously printing parts 24/7 ­ From [63]
2.4
Support Structure Removal
Support structures are often necessary when printing resin parts with VPP, as the liquid
resin in the vat alone cannot support the printed structure. Supports should not be broken
off the part manually, as this can lead to notches and small gouges on the part [66]. Cutting
pliers and blades can instead be used to detach supports, making cuts near the point of
contact. Sanding can further improve the surface finish after the part has fully cured.
Hobbyists on various forums have posted solutions showing how heating up the 3D prints
to soften the material and support structures makes it easier to remove. In an AM farm
setting, this technique could be used to remove the support during the drying step, where
heat already would be involved [67].
A computer numerically controlled (CNC) mill with an auto­generated path is used in other
industries for AM of metals [68]. This approach is limited to a certain type of geometries,
and might not be suitable for VPP prints with small features.
Other companies such as HeyGears provide solutions or modules aimed at decreasing
the need for support. The HeyGears UltraCraft Pulsing release module claims to reduce
the need for supports by up to 25% as seen in Figure 2.11.
Figure 2.11: HeyGears UltraCraft Pulsing Release Module support reduction ­ From [69]
The AM Farm project plan seen in Appendix A.4.3 also mentions gel­like interfaces be­
18
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:31==
tween the support and the part. This is an area that needs further research, but could
eventually lead to parts free from scarring due to supports.
2.5
Drying
There are numerous ways to dry the parts after being exposed to solvents. It is important
to dry the part before curing to avoid left­over solvent mixed with resin residue. Having a
dry part gives the best basis for dimensional accuracy. The following list shows some of
the available drying methods:
• Air Drying
– Placing the part in a well­ventilated area and letting it air dry
• Compressed Air
– Using compressed air to blow off excess solvent from the part.
• Vacuum Drying
– Evaporates and removes liquids due to low pressure
• Heat Drying
– Uses a heat source to evaporate solvents
A combination of these methods is also used to ensure sufficient drying [70].
2.6
Post­curing
Post­curing of VPP printed parts is essential to achieve their final mechanical properties,
including strength and stiffness. This process involves exposing the ’green’ parts (parts
that have been printed but not yet fully cured) to additional UV light or heat treatment.
The purpose of post­curing is to complete the polymerization process, ensuring that the
material reaches its optimal performance characteristics. During this phase, the uncured
monomers in the printed parts are further polymerized, leading to improved structural
integrity and overall durability of the parts.
The current methods used for post­curing resin parts traditionally employ flood light at
405 nm, although this depends on the specific photopolymer and photoinitiator used in
the resin. Most resins react to UV light at a wavelength of 405 nm, but some react to light
at 365 nm. Others resins also benefit from thermal curing. The curing time need for a part
to reach its optimal mechanical property depends on the resin and the size and geometry
of the part [71]. In an AM farm, the curing time must be calibrated to the individual part
size to ensure a proper cured final product.
2.7
AM Farm Settings
2.7.1
Build Plate Leveling
Each of the mounting mechanisms of the build plate discussed in Section 2.1 previously
still requires an independent leveling feature to ensure that the build plate is properly
aligned and parallel to the bottom of the vat. Leveling is typically done via either a ball­
joint connection or 4 adjustment screws; some newer models provide automatic leveling
features like the newest line of Anycubic’s Photon Mono printers [72]. Automatic build
plate leveling would be a highly beneficial printer feature, for an AM farm where multiple
build plates would be interchangeable between printers.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
19
==PAGE:32==
2.7.2
Maintenance and Resin Refilling
Implementing features such as automatic resin refilling in printer vats and a resin level
sensor in the refilling tank would provide significant benefits in a manufacturing environ­
ment. These features would ensure a continuous supply of resin, reducing the likelihood
of production interruptions. The resin level sensor would notify operators when a replace­
ment is necessary, allowing for timely refilling and preventing any delays. Moreover, this
system would improve productivity and reduce manual monitoring tasks, freeing up op­
erators for more strategic activities. Some printer manufacturers already provide resin
refilling systems, showcasing the feasibility and effectiveness of such implementations
[73, 74].
In addition, solvent monitoring in the cleaning vats with a digital hydrometer would bring
substantial advantages [75]. This technology could notify operators or even automated
robots about the need to exchange the solvent, ensuring optimal printer performance and
maintaining product quality. The digital hydrometer would provide accurate and real­time
data, assisting in maintaining the correct solvent concentrations.
Furthermore, integrating a manufacturing execution system (MES) to track things such
as the resin tanks (vats) and their print time would help estimate when an exchange or
maintenance is required. A clear overview of the usage of printer vats and re­exchanging
them prior to deterioration and puncturing, would facilitate predictive maintenance, reduc­
ing unexpected downtimes and improving overall operational efficiency.
2.7.3
Robotic Automation
Some manufacturers have implemented robotic solutions for build plate handling with
minor changes to their existing build plate mounting setup. Carbon3D did a proof­of­
concept using a SCARA robot [76] to move the build plate from the printer to a washing
unit. MaxResolution3D has in collaboration with Nexa3D implemented a collaborative
robot that moves the build plates to a washing unit [77]. Stratasys, in collaboration with
Calvary Robotics, has showcased a full production line named P3™Automated Produc­
tion Cell seen in Figure 2.12. This demonstration exemplifies how VPP technology can
be scaled to a factory­level solution. The system is equipped with an industrial robot arm
and a conveyor belt, efficiently handling all steps in the post­processing chain, except for
the removal of supports [78].
Figure 2.12: Stratasys P3™Automated Production Cell ­ From [78]
20
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:33==
3
System Requirements for the AM Farm
This chapter outlines the requirements for the AM farm, segmented into key components,
with the architecture detailed in Section 1.2 and research on the State of the Art of VPP
post­processing in Section 2 forming the baseline, where system­level requirements were
formed based on state­of­the­art insights, literature research, and the initial AM Farm
project plan [79]. Following the overall system requirements, the requirements for the
cleaning module will be presented, as it pertains to the scope of this project in particular.
3.1
System Level Requirements
These top­level system requirements follow the vernacular from table 3.1[80]. All require­
ments and specifications were continuously reviewed and updated throughout the devel­
opment process as new learning’s emerged and the definitions of each module became
more detailed.

Table 3.1: Vernacular of the requirement list
1. The AM farm shall be divided into kernels containing at least 10 VPP printers
each.
2. The AM farm system shall eliminate human contact with uncured resin and
solvents.
3. The AM farm shall minimize manual labor throughout the process chain.
4. The AM farm kernel shall include a universal mounting interface between build
plates, printers, and the post­processing modules.
5. The automatic handling system shall transfer build plates and parts with a
precision of at least ±0.5 mm in X, Y, and Z directions.
6. The AM farm shall minimize the use of solvent and waste stream.
7. Each printer within a kernel should use the same resin material for printing.
8. Each module, excluding the printers and post­curing, shall have a takt time at
or below 12 minutes per build plate.
9. Printed parts shall remain on the build plate until they are fully cleaned and
cured.
10. The AM farm system must be capable of manufacturing and handling any
printed part geometry, including the benchmark parts suggested in this the­
sis.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
21
==PAGE:34==
11. The post­processing modules must process parts to a quality standard com­
parable to or better than manual post­processing methods.
12. The post­processing system shall be modular and scalable in its architecture.
13. The transportation of the build plates from printers to the cleaning module
should be done by the automatic handling system.
14. The post­processing modules should have defined processes and timings for
each part and material to ensure optimal cleaning and curing procedures.
15. The AM Farm system shall monitor and control workflow in real time and pro­
vide alerts for any module failures.
The set requirement for the AM Farm design is aimed at optimizing efficiency, safety, and
quality in the AM process. Firstly, to ensure manageability and scalability, the AM Farm
shall be divided into kernels, each containing at least 10 VPP printers as specified by re­
quirement 1, this further enables the modularity requirement 12, allowing different printers
per kernel as well as scalability by increasing production capability per kernel. It is rec­
ommended for ease of operation that the resin used in a kernel is consistent but can vary
between kernels to provide a material portfolio for the customer. Following requirement
4, a universal mounting interface between build plates, printers, and the post­processing
modules is necessary, so cobots can move the build plate between modules. The printed
parts must remain on the build plate until fully cleaned and cured, according to require­
ment 9.
There are no fixed requirements for the VPP printers in each kernel, allowing for variability
and flexibility within the farm. This flexibility is beneficial as it permits the integration
of different types of VPP printers in various kernels, catering to diverse material needs
and production capabilities. Furthermore, it enables the AM Farm to stay current with
technological advancements. As new and improved printers are developed, individual
kernels can be upgraded without necessitating a complete overhaul of the entire system.
This modular approach not only enhances the scalability and manageability of the farm but
also ensures that the latest innovations in VPP printing can be seamlessly incorporated to
maintain optimal production efficiency and quality. Each module, except for the printers
and post­curing, must have a takt­time below 12 minutes per build plate, as stated in
requirement 8, in order to accommodate a short print time of down to 2 hours. Additionally,
to make the AM Farm more environmentally friendly and economically viable, it should
minimize solvent use and waste, set by requirement 6.
To ensure high standards, the automation of the AM Farm post­processing must deliver
quality that meets or surpasses the manual post­processing methods, per requirement
11. More importantly, the health risks imposed on working in a VPP AM farm must be
mitigated by the cleaning module which must ensure that no human operator has to deal
directly with resins and solvents, defined by the requirement 2. The collaborative robot
should handle the build plate transportation.
These requirements ensure that the AM Farm system can handle a diverse range of
printed part geometries and maintain high standards of quality, safety, and efficiency
throughout the production process.
These requirements also form the foundation for
a safe and automated post­processing system, as proposed in this thesis, which aims
to eliminate the need for human interaction with uncured resin, enhance safety, reduce
manual operations, and make the AM process more sustainable from both business and
environmental perspectives.
22
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:35==
After establishing the top­level requirements for an AM farm kernel, the focus now narrows
specifically to the Cleaning Module, details of which will be presented in the following
section and the main focus of this thesis.
3.2
Cleaning Module
A primary objective of the cleaning module in the AM Farm is to handle the printed parts
from the printers to a fully cleaned stage in an automatic manner. This approach ad­
dresses the dirty and hazardous aspects of VPP post­processing, ensuring that printed
parts are free from residual liquid resin while minimizing human intervention.
To achieve a sustainable AM Farm utilizing VPP technologies and photopolymers, min­
imizing solvent usage and resin waste is imperative. As production volume increases,
solvents and resins will become the primary variable costs. It is therefore important to
optimise the consumption of these solvents to achieve economic feasibility.
3.3
Cleaning Module Requirements
The following section will present the requirements for the cleaning module, based on the
scope of this thesis, and the objectives of the AM Farm research project.
1. The cleaning module must fully clean the part from uncured resin.
2. The printed parts including supports must stay adhered to the build plate dur­
ing cleaning.
3. The cleaning module should have implemented filters to remove debris, cured
particles, and contaminants.
4. The cleaning module should recycle as much of uncured resin as possible.
5. The cleaning module shall not be a bottleneck in the post­processing chain.
6. No post­curing should happen in the cleaning module.
7. The cleaning system should have custom cleaning cycles based on resin ma­
terial and print volume.
8. The cleaning system should incorporate sensors or feedback mechanisms to
trigger maintenance of the components.
9. The cleaning process must effectively remove uncured resin without damag­
ing or changing the geometry of the printed parts.
10. No human operator should not come in contact with solvent or resin.
There are many ways in which this set of requirements can be accommodated. Some
manufacturers perform a multi­stage residual resin removal process [54, 81], where the
first stage usually removes 85% to 90% of the resin in a preliminary wash or soak step.
The remaining resin 15% to 5% is removed in a finer wash in the second step [29]. To
enhance the economic and sustainability aspects of requirement 4, the cleaning process
was strategically divided into two distinct stages: Pre­cleaning and Final cleaning. This
division was implemented at the beginning of the project with the primary goal of min­
imising solvent use and resin waste. The Pre­cleaning step focuses on applying methods
for coarse cleaning without the use of solvents, thus enabling the recovery of resin and
adhering to requirement 4. The final cleaning step ensures that parts are completely free
from uncured resin, adhering to requirements: 1, 3 and 7. Requirements 2,5,6, 8,9 and
10 will be a shared set of requirements between the pre­ and final cleaning solution.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
23
==PAGE:36==
Figure 3.1: The focus of this thesis work, with the Cleaning module split into the Pre­
Cleaning and Final Cleaning segments.
The two­step cleaning process, illustrated in Figure 3.1 presents several benefits. Firstly,
it allows for resin recovery during the pre­cleaning stage, reducing material waste and
lowering operational costs. Secondly, it ensures thorough cleaning in the final stage,
enhancing the quality of the finished parts. Additionally, separating the cleaning process
into distinct stages can lead to better process control and optimisation opportunities.
However, the introduction of an additional module also has its drawbacks. It increases the
complexity of the AM Farm system, requiring more equipment, space, and maintenance.
Furthermore, it could lead to higher initial setup costs and longer processing times, which
need to be balanced against the operational benefits.
By carefully evaluating these trade­offs, the two­step cleaning approach aims to achieve
a sustainable and efficient cleaning process, balancing the economic and environmental
impacts while maintaining high­quality production standards.
24
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:37==
4
Materials and Methods
This chapter will present the fundamental materials and methods that support the devel­
opment work carried out in this thesis project. Firstly, it will be discussed how cleanliness
can be quantified. The printers used for manufacturing the parts used for testing and char­
acterising the cleaning modules built will be presented, alongside the Benchmark parts
used for testing cleaning performance. The chapter will also outline the basics of the
statistics concerning the Design of Experiments methodology utilised for testing cleaning
methods and machines.
4.1
Development and Resource Considerations
The general approach has been to use and utilise existing parts and components at the
AM Research and Development Lab at DTU. This was done in an effort to minimise the
cost and lead time of the components. The workshop in Building 427 of DTU has also con­
tributed significantly to custom­made parts, where milling, turning, EDM, bending, laser
cutting, aluminium profile cutting, and grinding operations have allowed the construction
of the machines. All of the sub­assemblies are made to be taken apart after assembly, for
ease of replacement and maintenance. Most of the standard components were bought
from RS Components due to fast delivery times and favourable DTU discounts [82].
4.2
Quantifying Cleanliness
The degree of cleanliness of the VPP AM parts is important as subpar cleaned parts could
be dimensionally inaccurate. Inaccuracy can increase the failure rate in applications of
the printed parts such as medical devices and electronics where tight tolerance chains are
present. Parts contaminated with residual resin also pose a health risk, as the contami­
nant can spread to skin or facial mucous membranes. Cleanliness refers to the absence
of residual resin, uncured resin, and contaminants on the printed parts. Several methods
to effectively quantify cleanliness are outlined below.
4.2.1
Visual Inspection
Visual inspection involves examining the surface of the printed parts under magnifica­
tion to identify residual resin and surface defects. While this method is straightforward,
it is subjective and may not detect all contaminants. Techniques like light optical mi­
croscopy (LOM) with great magnification provide more detailed surface analysis. This
method yields a qualitative measure of the cleanliness and can reveal the position of
residual resin by surfaces being vaguely shiny or having shimmering streaks.
4.2.2
Weight Measurement
Weight measurement before and after cleaning can quantify the amount of residual ma­
terial removed. By weighing the part at various stages, the effectiveness of the cleaning
process can be assessed [83]. This method yields a quantitative measure of the cleanli­
ness of the whole part, albeit it does not reveal the position of the residual resin.
4.2.3
Surface Analysis Techniques
Fourier­transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) and Raman spectroscopy can identify
chemical residues on the surface of the printed parts. These techniques detect spe­
cific molecular vibrations, revealing the presence of residual uncured resin [84, 85]. This
method yields a quantitative measure of the cleanliness for a very small area of the part.
It reveals the position of the residual resin in a small area.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
25
==PAGE:38==
4.2.4
Contact Angle Measurement
Contact angle measurement evaluates surface cleanliness by measuring the angle at
which a water droplet contacts the surface. A lower contact angle indicates better cleanli­
ness, as contaminants often alter surface wettability [86]. This method yields a qualitative
measure of cleanliness but could be quantitative for small areas if the material has been
characterised beforehand.
4.2.5
Choosing Cleanliness Quantifying Method
This is not an exhaustive list but gives an idea to what methods can be employed. As
a whole, the research area on quantitative cleanliness of VPP AM parts is deficient as
there is not any rigid standard that defines when a 3D printed part has been cleaned
sufficiently. For this project, weight measurement and visual inspection were chosen as
they were readily available and required little to no training. In the interest of time, these
two methods were deemed to be the best fitting for the project.
4.3

The Phrozen Mighty 8K, now referenced as Phrozen, has been used as the primary printer
for this project. It is an LCD printer with an XY­resolution of 28 μm and a minimum layer
thickness of 10 μm [87]. It is a medium­sized resin printer, with a print volume of 218 x 123

hindered with the stock brackets.

4.3.1
Print challenges
During the printing of a full build plate with scaled benchmark parts, it was observed that
one of the prints in the middle did not adhere to the build plate, while other prints had
severe surface defects. A small study was conducted to find the course of this issue after
the prints from both of the Phrozen printers showed similar issues. During the printing
process in VPP, a small vacuum is formed between the vat and the newly cured layer.
The peeling forces must be considered to avoid layer separation [92]. Figure 4.2 shows
different steps during the printing of a layer. A small vacuum forms between steps 4 and
5.
Figure 4.2: Bonding forces during VPP printing ­ From [5]
It is furthermore important that the lift speed of the stage matches the re­flow ability of the
used resin (viscosity). These two parameters must be adjusted to allow for a sufficient
amount of new resin to flow in under the printed part as it releases from the membrane to
alleviate the vacuum without compromising the printing time. The printing speed varies
from brand to brand, as different technologies are introduced [2]. As print time is currently
the longest step in the VPP AM process chain, it is crucial that the quality of the finished
prints meet expectations. Table 4.3 shows the changes in settings.
Old settings
New settings

1,73
2,01

60%
100%
Table 4.3: Comparison of old and new settings
Here, a 14% increase in print time led to 40% increase in success rate. These param­
eters must be carefully monitored in an industrial setting to find the optimal compromise
between quality and time for an AM farm.

4.4
Benchmark Parts
In order to test the cleaning performance of the cleaning module two Benchmark parts
were selected for this project.
The industry partners provided a range of parts, which were similar to what they would
expect to print. From the list in Table 4.1, the file ”top.step” was chosen to print in both
full­scale and smaller versions. Figure 4.4 shows different areas of the Top part. The
geometries are difficult to clean, which is used to assess the performance of the differ­
