4.5
Statistical Basis
In order to assess the results from the tests, a basis for understanding the statistical anal­
ysis is paramount. In the following section, the statistical basis for the analysis of the re­
sults will be presented. This section is in its entirety from two bodies of work: DTU Course
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
31
==PAGE:44==
02411 Statistical Design and Analysis of Experiments taught by Proff. Murat Külahci, us­
ing the work by Douglas Montgomery in the book: Design and Analysis of Experiments
9th Edition and the statistics course 02402 at Technical University of Denmark [94, 95].
4.5.1
Design of Experiments
Design of experiments (DoE) is a systematic method used in scientific and industrial re­
search to plan, conduct, analyze, and interpret controlled tests. The primary goal of DoE
is to determine the relationship between factors affecting a process and the output of that
process. This aids operators to optimize processes, improve quality, and make informed
decisions.
In order to understand DoE, the following are some key concepts that define the area:
1. Factors and Levels:
• Factors: These are the independent variables in an experiment that are be­
lieved to influence the response variable. Factors can be controllable (e.g.,
temperature, pressure) or uncontrollable (e.g., environmental conditions).
• Levels: These are the different values or settings that each factor can take. For
example, if the factor is time, the levels might be 5 min, 10 min, and 15 min.
2. Response Variable:
• This is the dependent variable that is measured in the experiment. It is the
outcome of interest that is influenced by the factors.
3. Treatment:
• A treatment is a specific combination of factor levels whose effect on the re­
sponse variable is to be determined.
4. Experimental Units:
• These are the individual subjects or items to which treatments are applied. For
example, in a clinical trial, patients are the experimental units.
There are many different kinds of experimental design and this project chose Full Factorial
Design (FFD) which investigates the effects of multiple factors simultaneously. Each factor
is studied at multiple levels, and all possible combinations of factor levels are included.
Statistical software
For all experiments performed, the statistical software JMP has been used to analyse
the results of the test data [96]. The software provides powerful tools for examining the
results of a DoE. In particular, JMP was instrumental in analysing the data from DoE’s from
the Pre­Cleaning module and the Final Cleaning module, performed in this study. The
software enabled a detailed examination of the data using techniques such as regression
analysis, ANOVA, and multivariate analysis. These analyses allowed for a comprehensive
understanding of the effects of different variables on the cleaning efficacy.
4.5.2
ANOVA
ANOVA (Analysis of Variance) is a statistical method used to test the differences between
three or more group means to determine if at least one of the means is significantly dif­
ferent from the others. Hypothesis testing in ANOVA involves the following steps:
1. Formulate a hypothesis
2. Calculate the test statistic (F­statistic)
3. Conclude if the data supports the null hypothesis
32
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:45==
4. Test assumptions
When formulating a hypothesis, it is typically done by stating that there are no differences
between a group of means (Null hypothesis). It is formulated as the following:
H0 : µ1 = µ2 = · · · = µk
H1 : At least one µi is different
Where H0 states that all means are equal (No means are different), or at least one mean
is statistically different. When deciding which hypothesis to believe, there are two types
of error that can occur: Type I errors and Type II errors.
Type I Error:
A Type I error occurs when the null hypothesis (H0) is true but is incorrectly rejected. This
is also known as a ”false positive”.
The significance level of the test, denoted by α is the probability of making a Type I error.
It represents the threshold at which we reject the null hypothesis.
P(Type I error) = α
A threshold of α=0.05 (5% chance of rejecting the null hypothesis when it is actually true)
is typically used and will be used as the threshold for this report.
Type II Error:
A Type II error occurs when the null hypothesis (H0) is false but is failed to be rejected.
This is also known as a ”false negative”.
The probability of making a Type II error is denoted by β. It represents the likelihood of
failing to detect a true effect or difference.
Power of a test:
The power of a test, denoted by 1 −β, is the probability of correctly rejecting the null hy­
pothesis when it is false. High power means a lower chance of committing a Type II error.
Typically the power of a test is chosen to be 80%.
Figure 4.10: Illustration of the relationship between the constituents of the null­hypothesis
­ From [97]
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
33
==PAGE:46==
Figure 4.10 illustrates the relationships between the constituents of the null hypothesis
[98].
ANOVA:
ANOVA involves partitioning the total variation observed in the data into components at­
tributable to different sources of variation. The F­statistic is used to determine whether
the observed variances are significantly different.
4.5.3
Assumptions of ANOVA
When performing ANOVA analysis, some inherent assumptions needs to be verified to
conclude if the analysis is valid. These are the assumptions:
1. Independence of Observations: The observations within each group must be in­
dependent of each other.
2. Normality: The residuals (differences between observed and predicted values)
should be approximately normally distributed within each group.
3. Homogeneity of Variances (Homoscedasticity): The variances among the groups
should be approximately equal.
To ensure that the observations are independent, i.e. observations in a group are not
related to each other, careful test design is typically considered: Random sampling and
random assignment of subjects to groups.
To check if the residuals are normally distributed N(µ, σ2), there are three methods that
can be employed:
1. Q­Q Plot (Quantile­Quantile Plot): This is a graphical method to assess if the
residuals follow a normal distribution. If the residuals lie approximately along the
reference line, the normality assumption is satisfied.
2. Shapiro­Wilks Test: This statistical test evaluates the null hypothesis that the resid­
uals come from a normal distribution. A non­significant result (p­value > 0.05) sug­
gests that the residuals are normally distributed.
3. Histograms: Plotting histograms of the residuals can also provide a visual assess­
ment of normality.
In this project, the Q­Q plot and Shapiro­Wilks test were used to confirm that the residuals
are normally distributed. The Shapiro­Wilks test alone would suffice, but the Q­Q plot
offers an easy to interpret, as can be observed in figure 4.11
34
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:47==
Figure 4.11: Example of a Q­Q plot with actual data from the tests conducted in this report.
It illustrates that the residuals all approximately fall on the reference line indicating that
the residuals are normally distributed.
To check the homogeneity of variances three methods can be employed:
1. Levene’s Test: This statistical test evaluates the null hypothesis that the variances
are equal across groups. A non­significant result (p­value > 0.05) indicates that the
variances are homogenous.
2. Bartlett’s Test: Similar to Levene’s test, it evaluates whether the variances are
equal. It is more sensitive to deviations from normality.
3. Boxplots: Visual inspection of boxplots for each group can help identify differences
in variance. If the spread of the boxes is similar, it suggests homogeneity of vari­
ances.
In this project, Levene’s test was chosen as the method of verifying homoscedasticity. It
is more flexible for datasets where it is uncertain if the data is normally distributed. If the
dataset is known to be normally distributed, it is better to use Bartlett’s test as it offers
more statistical power for datasets that are known to be normally distributed.
4.5.4
Post­Hoc method
After conducting an ANOVA test, if the null hypothesis is rejected, it indicates that there
is a significant difference among the group means. However, ANOVA does not specify
which groups are different. Post­hoc tests are used to determine exactly which groups
have significant differences. Here a commonly used Post­Hoc method is presented:
Tukey’s Honestly Significant Difference (HSD) Test
Tukey’s HSD test is a pairwise comparison method that controls the overall Type I error
rate and is designed for comparing all possible pairs of means.
Tukey’s HSD test is useful for all­pairwise comparisons. There are other post­hoc meth­
ods, which were considered for this project, but were deemed too conservative. When
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
35
==PAGE:48==
choosing which post­hoc method to use, it is important to strike a balance between the
probability of making type I and type II errors.
For this project, Tukey’s HSD test was chosen as the post­hoc method because it is less
conservative than the Bonferroni correction and Holm’s method, and yields results that
are more simple to interpret for datasets with equal sample sizes. In Figure 4.12 it can
be observed how the interpretation of Tukey’s HSD test results is simple to interpret, as
groups with overlapping confidence intervals are deemed to not be sufficiently different.
Figure 4.12: Example of a Tukey’s HSD test plot from the actual data in this report. Groups
with overlapping confidence intervals are deemed to not be sufficiently different
36
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:49==
5
Universal Mounting Interface
A key enabler for the automation of the AM Farm and the integration of various post­
processing modules is the interface that couples the build plate to the printer, clean­
ing modules, and the remainder of the AM farm post­processing system. The currently
available solutions for build plate mounting were investigated in Section 2.1, where none
proved to be optimal for automating the workflow in an AM Farm. In order to enable the in­
terface of build plates to the post­processing system, an MVP automation solution needed
to be developed.
The following chapter will outline the requirements for a universal mounting interface and
present a suggested solution. This solution aims to facilitate automation and robotic han­
dling for subsequent modules involved in pre­cleaning and final cleaning.
5.1
Requirements
Micrometer­level precision during mounting is essential for optimal initial layer adhesion
in VPP. High accuracy in vertical position and angular alignment is crucial for successful
prints. The specific functional requirement for this interface follows the necessities for au­
tomation and is further inspired by the AM Farm project plan Appendix A.4.3 and research
by Artemeva et al. [27] on kinematic coupling mechanisms.
1. The build plate shall be able to be picked up automatically in a consistent manner
by a robot arm.
2. The coupling mechanism for handling build plates must be durable and secure, en­
suring precise alignment and preventing any possibility of misalignment or dropping
during operation.
3. The handling system should be reliable and require minimal maintenance.
4. The universal mounting interface should have a positional precision of 15 µm in the
Z­direction.
5. The universal coupling mechanism should have a positional precision of 1 mm in
the X­ and Y­direction.
6. The universal coupling should be usable for both a robot arm as well as a human
operator.
7. The kinematic coupling should be optimally constrained in all Degrees of Freedom
when fully mounted.
Only high positional requirements are established for the Z­direction, since it is essential
for initial layer adhesion in VPP. However, for X­ and Y­direction of the mounting a 1 mm
offset in position is allowed since the tolerances between the build plate and the vat are
rather large, and the printing screen is usually bigger than the build plate. To ensure a
streamlined and agile operation with and without collaborative robots in place, the uni­
versal mounting interface is required to work with both a cobot and a human operator,
determined by the requirement 6.
With the requirements for the universal mounting interface settled, subsequent develop­
ment and investigation could be initiated.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
37
==PAGE:50==
5.2
One­step Mounting Concept
All previously discussed build plate mounting solutions operated through a two­step pro­
cess. First, the build plate is mounted on the printer’s arm/vertical axis, and then it is se­
cured in place via mechanisms such as cam locks, screw connections, or linear solenoids.
In order to integrate automation by the use of a cobot, without inducing more complexity
and systems to be integrated with the printer and robot arm, a one­step mounting solution
was investigated. The concept of a one­step is inspired by compliant mechanisms, where
an element in the kinematic chain is flexible and enables the mounting to flex and then
snap into place. Compliant mechanisms are particularly useful for creating systems with
fewer moving parts and greater reliability [99].
A one­step solution is suitable for implementing a simple and easy­to­handle mechanism
for a collaborative robot, with fewer failure points, thus mitigating some risk of failure
during the mounting and dismounting sequences in production runs. However, the use of
a collaborative robot as an automated handling system will require a forceful movement
to overcome the flexing force of the compliant mechanism, which can be programmed
into the robot’s movement pattern [100].
Early in the development process, a one­step solution inspired by the design proposal
from Artemeva et al. [27] was explored. Specifically, the locking ball mechanism was
tested to determine if it could work with a spring providing the necessary locking force to
secure the mounting plate, rather than using a push­pull solenoid. Initial testing revealed
that the locking balls were prone to jamming and the spring­loaded forces needed to act
on the balls made it difficult to assemble and service. As a result, the idea was not pursued
further.
A modification for implementing a one­step automation­friendly mounting solution on the
printer was prototyped when the Phrozen printers became available for this project [87].
The Phrozen printer originally has a standard M8 thumbscrew for securing the build plate
in position, which can be seen in Figure 5.1. The intent was to use the stock vertical axis
but modify the build plate mount and screw for a one­step solution.
Figure 5.1: The stock build plate mounting on the Phrozen printer ­ From [101]
By adding a pressure disc with a spring on top secured in place with a M8 screw, this
sub­assembly could serve as the compliant mechanism. The build plate could then be
secured in the Z­direction by the spring force. On the build plate mount a trapeze shape
was added to make the build plate snap and stay in position (X­axis) once mounted. The
initial prototype 3D printed by FDM can be seen in Figure 5.2. Different impact angles of
38
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:51==
the trapezoidal shape were made to ensure non­symmetric pull forces when pushing or
pulling, to ensure the mount would stay properly locked in place during printing operations.
Figure 5.2: First FDM printed prototype for universal kinematic coupling
The reason for continuing to use a mounting screw on top of the spring is to enable ad­
justment of spring­loaded force and find an optimum of compression allowing the build
plate to be mounted and dismounted with a firm motion while still providing enough force
in its resting position to have the build plate locked in place for successful printing.
5.3
One­step Mount Design
After having functionally tested the initial mount, a proof­of­concept was established, and
a simple iteration was made to further optimise the mounting and dismounting angles on
the trapezoidal shape. The mounting and dismounting angles were updated to 20 and
30 degrees, respectively. This iteration was then manufactured in resin on the Phrozen
printer itself, the taper angles were validated, but the 20­degree mounting slope was ex­
tended, as seen in Figure 5.3a to make the engagement of the pressure disc smoother.
Minor changes were implemented to the pressure disc design to improve the stability and
functionality of the mechanism. The pressure disc was ensured to interface with the 30­
degree ramp on the build plate mount, when fully mounted, to ensure there was no play.
The final design of the pressure disc can be seen in figure 5.3b.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
39
==PAGE:52==
(a) Build plate
(b) Final Pressure Disc design
Figure 5.3: One­step mount design
After the latest design of this one­step mechanism was tested and validated, the subse­
quent version was manufactured in the workshop in 427. The pressure disc was turned in
POM for smooth operation, as POM has self­lubrication properties [102]. The build plate
mount was manufactured from aluminium to balance structural integrity and machinability.
The final implemented design of the universal mounting interface, when mounting on the
printer can be seen in Figure 5.4. It additionally features custom leveling brackets and a
flat­milled aluminium build plate, manufactured by the workshop in DTU building 427.
The technical drawings for the final iteration of the build plate mount and pressure disc
can be found in Appendix A.1.
Figure 5.4: Universal Mounting interface with build plate mounted on the Phrozen printer.

5.3.1
Force Calculations for Build Plate Mounting
To ensure proper design and functionality, the forces required to mount and dismount the
build plate were calculated. The mounting sequence for the build plate can be seen in
Figure 5.5.
Figure 5.5: Build Plate mount sequence
Parameters
The necessary parameters for calculations of forces required for mounting, and dismount­
ing where friction is disregarded are:
• Spring constant (k):
A 4.510 N/mm compression spring was used as the compliant element [103]
• Pre­compression of the spring (x):
The M8 mounting screw was tightened such that the spring was compressed by
8 mm, an adjustable compression.
• Mounting compression of the spring (∆x):
The spring needs to compress additionally 5 mm to snap in and out of position.
• Angle of the ramp for mounting (θm):
Mounting angle is 20◦
• Angle of the ramp for dismounting (θd):
Dismounting angle is 30◦
Spring compression Force (Fspring)
A pre­compression of the spring at 8 mm and an additional compression of 5 mm results
in a total spring compression of 13 mm for the mounting and dismounting sequences.
The force the spring then provides is given by:
Spring force = k · x = 4.51(N/mm) · 13 mm = 58.63N
(5.1)
Resulting Mounting forces
For θm = 20◦(Mounting):
Fmove, mount = Fspring · sin(20◦) = 20.04 N
(5.2)
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
41
==PAGE:54==
For θd = 30◦(Dismounting):
Fmove, dismount = Fspring · sin(30◦) = 26.50 N
(5.3)
Build Plate Positional Holding Force
The force holding the build plate in place when neglecting the friction is based on a spring
compression of 5 mm + 3.75 mm, there the 5 mm comes from the pre­compression and
the 3.75 is the resting high of the pressure disc when the build plate is mounted:
Fspring, hold = k · x = 4.51 N/mm · 8.75 mm = 39.49 N
(5.4)
One concern by using a spring force to secure the build plate in place during a print in VPP
forces have been shown to exceed 200 N [5, 104]. However, these high forces, which
exceed 200 N, are applied only in tension. To ’release’ the build plate the force acting on
the spring would need to be in compression. The compression forces during printing are
significantly lower, and the spring force would prevail over the applied force.
5.4
Robot Interface
The universal coupling mechanism was implemented with minimal changes to the existing
Phrozen printer. No robotic­specific interfaces were added for gripping the build plate.
The flat sides and front protrusions (Figure 5.6a) provide a fairly sufficient interface for an
adaptive gripper system, such as the 2F­85 Adaptive Gripper from Robotiq seen in Figure
5.6b. With this interface style, the flat sides serve as the primary contact faces, while the
front protrusions act as end­stops, enabling the gripper to efficiently pull the build plate
from the printer and post­processing systems.
(a) Custom mount
(b) Robotiq Adaptive Gripper ­ From [105]
Figure 5.6: Custom Build plate mount and gripper for interfacing
5.5
Testing
The universal mounting interface developed from the stock Phrozen printers was tested
in operation, to see if it successfully would hold the build plate in place during a print. The
test was done by levelling the build plate, then performing a sequence of 10 mounting
42
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:55==
and dismounting sequences. After the (dis)mounting sequence a print was started, which
ultimately came out successful. However, it was noticed that with the large forces, when
separating the first few burn­in layers from the membrane the mount would rotate slightly
around the Z­axis since the mount was not fully constrained in Z rotation, which can be
referenced in Figure 5.6a. This caused layer shifting for burn­in layers, but since the first
layers only acted as the supporting raft, the rest of the print came out successfully. A
quick fix for eliminating the tolerance which allowed rotation around the Z­axis was to put
a piece of duct tape on the printer’s Z­stage arm, which made the mount fitting onto the z­
stage to a very slight compression fit. Of­course, an iteration and change in design would
be better to remedy the issue of fully constraining the build plate mount on the printer arm,
which will be shortly discussed in the following section.
The universal mounting interface, developed from the stock Phrozen printers, was tested
to ascertain its ability to securely hold the build plate in place during printing operations.
The testing process involved levelling the build plate and performing a sequence of 10
dismounting and mounting cycles. After this sequence, a print was initiated, which ulti­
mately resulted in a successful print outcome. However, it was observed that during the
separation of the burn­in layers from the membrane, the mount experienced slight rotation
around the Z­axis, due to the high forces [104]. This was because the mount was inad­
equately constrained in this DoF. This rotation caused a minor layer shift in the burn­in
layers. Despite this, since the first layers were merely the raft, the remainder of the print
was unaffected and came out successfully.
To address the issue of rotational play around the Z­axis, a quick fix was implemented
by applying a piece of duct tape to the printer’s Z­stage arm, effectively eliminating the
undesired movement. Nevertheless, a more permanent solution involving a redesign to
fully constrain the build plate mount on the printer arm is recommended. This problem
and potential designs for its mitigation will be discussed in the following section.
5.6
Future Work and Design
In spite of an inadequately constrained universal mounting interface, the compliant mech­
anism approach seems to lend its merits on it simplicity and robustness. It is recom­
mended to continue iterating on the principal design, and implement design features from
machine tooling, using tapered and keyed design, such that all the DoF are robustly con­
strained and accurately position the build plate every time.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
43
==PAGE:56==
6
Pre­Cleaning
The Pre­Cleaning module is a significant addition to the AM Farm project, aimed at en­
hancing energy efficiency and cost competitiveness compared to injection moulding. Its
primary purpose is to recover and reuse resin adhering to printed parts through surface
tension and the inherent stickiness of photopolymers. Theoretically, the recovered resin
can be recycled back into the printers, leading to economic benefits. By mechanically re­
moving the resin from printed parts on the build plate, the module also decreases solvent
usage, enabling prolonged solvent effectiveness for each cleaned build plate.
6.1
Pre­Cleaning Module Requirements
The Pre­Cleaning module is not designed to output completely clean parts but aims to
remove a substantial percentage of resin as detailed in the module’s requirements. The
requirements for the Pre­Cleaning module are as follows:
1. The Pre­Cleaning module must remove at least 60% of the uncured resin from
the parts.
2. The Pre­Cleaning module must not damage or deform the parts.
3. The Pre­Cleaning module must work with the parts still on the build plate.
4. The Pre­Cleaning module shall be compatible with the universal kinematic
coupling mechanism for attaching build plates.
5. The Pre­Cleaning module should collect and enable recycling of the uncured
resin.
6. The Pre­Cleaning module shall be compatible with build plates up to 218 mm
in the X­direction.
7. The Pre­Cleaning module shall be compatible with build plates up to 123 mm
in the Y­direction.
8. The Pre­Cleaning module shall be compatible with parts up to 60 mm in the
Z­direction as seen in Table 4.1.
9. The Pre­Cleaning module must be modular, to secure future upgrades and
compatibility.
The scouting of existing technologies and solutions combined with the set requirements
provides the framework for generating ideas for the Pre­Cleaning module.
6.2
Concept Generation
It is evident from the state­of­the­art section, that few solutions exist, which would fit the
requirements listed in the introduction. The main challenge for this module is finding
a way to remove the excess resin without damaging the parts. The solution must be
implementable across geometries varying in size, shape, and quantity and should support
the final cleaning module. A brainstorm based on the requirements led to the following
ideas:
• Passive drip ­ A cheap and simple way to let the excess resin drip from the build
plate into a container, where it could be collected for later use.
44
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:57==
• Vacuum ­ Remove resin from blind holes and difficult geometries
• Vibration plate ­ Assist dripping with a vibrating plate to speed up the process
• Centrifuge ­ Attempt to remove resin from parts using centrifugal forces.
• Air Blade removal ­ Using compressed air to create an air knife capable of removing
excess resin.
• Tumble finishing ­ Use an absorbing media to remove resin from the parts during
tumbling.
• Brush cleaning ­ Using soft brushes to mechanically remove resin
A Pugh matrix was made to choose which concepts to proceed with [106]. Here, the
passive drip was chosen as the baseline, as it is the simplest idea.

6.2.1
Concept Testing
The chosen concepts were implemented in crude prototypes. This methodology wasdone
to, determine whether a concept is worth pursuing further. The following prototypes were
developed for the pre­cleaning module:
Passive drip
The passive drip was tested using the substitute resin described in Section 6.3. Here,
a build plate with benchmark parts was submerged in the substitute resin and placed
directly in a container on a scale. The build plate was then lifted after 5, 10, and 20
minutes to determine how much resin substitute had dripped into the container. This test
was carried out with the build plate laying flat in a horizontal position and with one standing
in its corner at a 40° inclination. Figure 6.1 shows the results from the two orientations of
the build plate.

The results follow similar curves, but it was evident that an inclined build plate lost more
excess resin than the one placed flat. The effect of dripping stagnates after 10 minutes,
which indicates that limited benefits are to be expected from letting the prints drip for
longer.
Spinning with a column drill
Figure 6.2a shows the first very simple centrifuge, consisting of an empty filament spool,
angle brackets, steel wire, and a FDM print to interface the threaded rod with the spool.
(a) Concept test of the centrifuge
(b) Parts dipped in syrup
Figure 6.2: First simple centrifuge concept test with parts dipped in resin
46
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:59==
The parts were dipped in maple syrup as a resin substitute and spun in a column drill. The
speed was varied, but it was not verified by the tachometer. A visual inspection after each
run showed how increasing the speed of the drill proved to remove more of the syrup.
The parts before and after spinning can be seen in Figure 6.3.
(a) Parts soaked in resin substitute
(b) Parts after spinning
Figure 6.3: Parts before and after first concept test
The incremental speed increase ultimately led to failure of the filament spool causing the
bracket to break off. This imbalance resulted in the bending of the rod connected to the
drill causing a catastrophic failure. This failure showed the importance of balance and
durability of the components, which will be considered moving forward.
This test was
quick to conduct and provided valuable insight and promising results.
Air Blade
The air pressure removal of resin using air proved to be problematic for several reasons.
The resin was sticking well to the surface of the part, the jet of air was difficult to get
under the part, and it was very difficult to clean blind holes where the resin could get
trapped. Moreover, to blow off resin from the surface of the part a high level of air pressure
was needed at which level the parts were prone to flying off or breaking. Additionally, a
hairdryer was tested with a custom nozzle to imitate an air blade commonly known from
Dyson, this was tested to see if the heat from the hairdryer could lower the viscosity of
the resin and make it easier to blow off, but it showed no significant impact.
6.2.2
Concept selection
The findings from the concept testing showed promising results for the centrifuge. This
concept will be developed further to test its potential for removing excess resin. Dripping
was also found as an effective way to remove the worst resin stuck, but it lacked the ability
to keep removing resin. Dripping is an inherent part of the VPP process and is therefore
also included when examining the centrifuge further.
6.3
Resin substitute
A safe, cheap, and available alternative to resin was investigated for the initial testings of
the Pre­Cleaning module. First, a low­viscosity maple syrup was used to coat the parts.
This approach was useful for the very first test, but both viscosity and density did not
match well with the resin used for printing.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
47
==PAGE:60==
To create a more accurate alternative in larger quantities, a mixture of water and soap was
used. Figure 6.4 shows the use of a sessile drop test on an inclined surface, a substitute
was examined next to a resin droplet, both containing 1 ml [107].
(a) Application of 1 ml drops
(b) Incline test
(c) Mixing in 5 liter container
Figure 6.4: Testing of resin substitute vs. actual resin on inclined plane and mixing
After multiple tests, a mixture of 10 parts soap and 1 part water was decided as the closest
behavior to the actual resin. This was seen in both the flow speed and width of the droplet,
indicating a similar surface tension. Figure 6.4c shows the mixing of the substitute in a
5­liter container. These quantities of substitutes were made after verifying the correct mix.
The ability to create large quantities of cheap, safe, and accurate resin substitutes allowed
for complete soaking of the parts instead of manual coating, which improved consistency
and saved time during testing. A custom dripping station was made to fit the dimensions
of the build plate and allow for repeatable dripping.
Green food coloring was added to the mix afterward for better visual inspection of the
parts. Later in the testing stages, glucose syrup was added to the soap mixture and
rebalanced to match the resin properties. This was done to mimic the sticky nature of the
resin.
6.4
Centrifuge ­ Concept Validation
In order to validate the feasibility centrifuge concept for a more industrial setting and con­
firm the possibility of spinning parts while adhered to the build plate, a prototype devel­
opment loop was initiated to examine whether this centrifugal method was useful for odd­
sized prints.
Centrifuge ­ Prototype
The enclosure for the first real centrifuge prototype was a vacuum chamber. The only
other centrifuge seen before, for VPP post­processing, was the one developed by Car­
bon3D, which is used exclusively for very shallow prints (approximately 15 mm Z­height)
directly on the platform [60], where air resistance would not be as significant. Therefore,
the vacuum chamber was initially selected due to concerns that air resistance on more
protruding prints with support structures might force the prints off the build plate. Utilizing
a vacuum could effectively eliminate this issue. The chamber also provided safety to the
48
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:61==
users in case of prototype failure. A top­loaded design with the motor attached through
the lid was chosen to avoid tampering with the vacuum chamber. The 300 mm diameter
of the chamber seen in Figure 6.5 shows the limited space available.
(a) Rendering of first centrifuge prototype
(b) Physical version of the prototype
Figure 6.5: Centrifuge prototype ­ From CAD to physical prototype
It was decided that the centrifuge should be able to support 3 build plates from the Elegoo
Saturn printers, which were abundantly available in the lab. The size of these build plates
is 122 mm x 196 mm, with its build volume illustrated in Figure 6.6 [108].
Figure 6.6: Build Volume for Elegoo Saturn LCD printer ­ From [108]
The size of the build plates determined the dimensions for many of the custom compo­
nents. 20x20 aluminium profiles were mounted in a U­shape, where the build plates can
slide in. This was implemented after the first iteration required disassembly to get the
build plates out. Figure 6.7 shows a cross­section of the prototype in CAD.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
49
==PAGE:62==
Figure 6.7: Cross section of prototype from CAD
The bottom plate, the spacers, and the yellow corner plugs are printed with PLA on a Cre­
ality Ender 3 Pro and Bambulab X1 FDM printer [109] [7]. Having the corners connected
and equally spaced towards the center provided a sturdy construction, where the build
plates were easy to remove.
For centrifuge calculations the relative centrifugal force (RCF) is often used to express
the experienced acceleration in g­forces to an accuracy within 0,1 % [110]. Equation 6.1
shows the derived final equation for the radius in centimeters.
RCF = r · 4 · π2 · (RPM)2
g · 3600
= 1, 118 · 10−5 · r · (RPM)2
(6.1)
The radius of the centrifuge can be changed between iterations but is difficult to change
after the final build. This means RPM is the most effective way to control RCF as it is also
a second­degree term.
The prototype showed that both spin duration and speed seemed to impact the cleanliness
of the parts. These two factors as well as the effect of drip time before spinning are
considered valuable when testing the capabilities of the prototype. Using a full factorial
DoE as described in Section 4.5 gives a systematic approach to testing, and examines
all possible combinations of the test.
The centrifuge is driven by a Makita DHP486 power drill, which has 2­speed levels [111].
The maximum speed at level 1 is rated at 550 RPM with no load and was measured at
530 RPM with load using a Lazmin DT­2234c+ digital tachometer [112]. This is used as
one of the values for the DoE. The next value of 1100 RPM was achieved using speed
50
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:63==
level 2 and a zip tie to reliably press the power drill trigger the same every time. This value
was also verified using the tachometer.
The first draft of a DoE for the centrifuge prototype is presented in Table 6.2. The goal
of this test was to determine if the centrifuge could work as an effective tool for removing
excess resin. A distance of 8 centimeters has been used to calculate the RCF based on
the given RPM according to equation 6.1. This distance is found from the center of mass
of the parts on the build plate to the center of rotation.

Figure 6.9: Different stages of the first Benchy centrifuge tests
These stages highlight the potential for the centrifuge. Here, it also becomes clear that
after dripping is no longer effective, spinning of the part removes an additional part of the
excess resin. This resin would have been waste, if not for the pre­cleaning of the part.
52
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:65==
Figure 6.11 shows the results of the 12 runs. These runs were completed in succession
using the following methodology:
1. Print and wash parts using standard protocol from Section 2.2.4.
2. Weigh the dry and clean parts.
3. Dip parts in resin substitute and measure weight after dripping for 5­10 min.
4. Spin for 30 seconds and weigh build plate.
5. Put build plate back in centrifuge and repeat step 4.
6. Repeat step 5 but now spin for 60 seconds for a total of 120 seconds. Weigh build
plate again
7. Repeat step 3 and repeat the process with new parameters for RPM.
Figure 6.10 shows the different stages of the parts. The clean state is achieved using the
standard cleaning protocol. The clean value serves as a benchmark throughout the test
and DoEs not fluctuate. The pre­spin state where the resin substitute is added is found
after dripping. This state can vary depending on dipping consistency and drip time. The
post­spin state is found after running the centrifuge and measuring. The responses from
the DoE are remaining resin and efficiency. The remaining resin should be minimized and
is calculated as seen in Equation 6.5
Remaining resin = Part weight post spin −Clean weight
(6.5)
The efficiency should be maximized and is calculated as seen in Equation 6.6.
Efficiency [%] = (Removed resin
Added resin
) ∗100
(6.6)
The efficiency is a powerful metric as it takes the fluctuating pre­spin state into consider­
ation. The remaining resin % is used for visual purposes to show how much resin is left
after each run. It is simply calculated using Equation 6.7.
Remaining resin [%] = (1 −Efficiency) ∗100
(6.7)
Figure 6.10: Process from clean state to post­spin state through resin addition and spin­
ning
The following method describes how the parts were coated and measured:
1. Parts are cleaned using the DTU standard protocol
2. The clean parts are weighed and this weight now serves as a benchmark
3. Parts are coated in resin substitute
4. Parts are weighed after dripping for 5­10 minutes
5. Parts are subjected to the cleaning method described in the DoE
6. Parts are weighed after spinning

Figure 6.11 shows the results from the prototype runs with the Benchy build plate. There
are no error bars on the figure, as there was only one build plate with the Benchy parts.
Future tests will run with 3 similar build plates for more reliable results.
Figure 6.11: Results from the prototype test with the Benchy build plate
It is important to note that the scale used, Rhewa 940C/20, only had an accuracy of
± 1 gram, which introduces a risk of rounding errors, especially since the parts are so
light [116]. Future testing will be done with a higher accuracy scale to minimize this source
of error.
Here, it shows how the change from 530 to 1100 RPM resulted in significantly more resin
substitute removal. Higher RPM was also attempted, but the prototype was not dimen­
sioned for this and the attempt was terminated for safety reasons.
It was also decided
that future tests must be dipped in substitute resin between each run to make them inde­
pendent from the previous runs. The overall results were promising, and it was decided
to proceed with the centrifuge as a Pre­Cleaning module.
6.5
Centrifuge Design
The promising results from the prototype require further investigation. A new set of re­
quirements is formulated before designing and constructing the MVP centrifuge. This is
once again done in an effort to constrain the design space and end up with a machine
that can be used to gather data and fulfill the requirements for an MVP module in the AM
Farm. The requirements can be seen in the following list:
1. The centrifuge must be able to fit multiple build plates in it.
2. The centrifuge must spin off the resin.
3. The centrifuge must not deform or damage the parts in any way.
54
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:67==
4. The printed parts must stay adhered to the build plate during its cycle.
5. The centrifuge must be compatible with a robot arm.
6. The centrifuge should collect the uncured resin and make it possible to recycle.
7. The centrifuge must be able to spin parts with Z­height up to 60 mm as described
in Table 4.1.
8. The centrifuge’s function shall not be impacted by uncured resin.
9. The centrifuge should have UV protection, in order to not cure resin.
10. Build plates must be mounted firmly into the centrifuge.
11. The centrifuge should be capable of reaching RCF values of 200.
12. The centrifuge must be able to accelerate to the desired RPM within 15 seconds.
13. The centrifuge should comply with the Machine Directive 2006/42/EC [117].
The biggest design changes moving forward from the prototype come from the require­
ments and the attempt to make the machine compatible with a robot arm. These require­
ments will be used to ensure the performance, safety, and durability of the centrifuge
design.
The engineering basis for this MVP solution is presented in the following chapters.
6.5.1
Design considerations
The following design considerations helped shape the outcome of the MVP centrifuge.
The requirements combined with the findings from the prototype provide a foundation for
the following:
• Decoupling ­ It was decided to pursue a design where individual components could
be substituted without compromising the design [118]. This eliminates joining meth­
ods such as welding, riveting, and gluing.
• Robot arm access ­ The robot arm should be able to move as freely and effectively as
possible in the AM Farm. It was therefore decided that the centrifuge is top­loaded
with no obstructions above the mounting point for the build plate.
• Belt driven ­ It was decided to drive the centrifuge with an electric motor using belts
and pulleys. This was done to reduce noise, make it possible to quickly swap pulleys
to change the gear ratio, and allow for different motors to power the system.
• Resin collection ­ It was decided to collect resin from the lowest point in the chamber,
which would be controlled by an inclined false bottom of the centrifuge chamber.
All of these considerations led to the final design, which can be seen in CAD in Figure
6.12a, and the physical design of the centrifuge in Figure 6.12b.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
55
==PAGE:68==
(a) Final centrifuge build ­ CAD
(b) Physical Centrifuge build
Figure 6.12: Digital and physical versions of centrifuge builds
The design consists of a top­loaded centrifuge with a detachable chamber made from an
oil drum. This sits on top of a base of aluminium profiles containing the brackets, bearings,
motor, power supply, and belt­tensioning required to run the centrifuge. The central shaft
is supported by two square flanged ball bearings of the UCF 200 series capable of 5850
RPM for the UCF 205 used for the 25 mm shaft diameter [119]. The shaft is connected
to a 25 mm thick aluminium ”flywheel”, which serves as a place for mounting as well as
providing stability to the system during spinning [120]. Appendix A.5, A.4, and A.10 shows
the technical drawings for the shaft, flange, and flywheel which are all custom­made in
the 427 workshop. Figure 6.13 shows the setup used throughout testing.
Figure 6.13: The rotating shaft supported by UFC 200 series bearings
56
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:69==
These UCF flanged bearings used have H6 tolerances [119]. The workshop was also
able to provide a shaft with h6 tolerance as seen in Appendix A.10. This component was
provided in 304 stainless steel. The h7 tolerance for the smaller diameter of the shaft
interfacing with a UFC 202 bearing was chosen, as it is turned to specification in the
workshop. This also allows for slightly easier assembly. The same was done on the other
end where the flange interfaces with a h7/H7 fit. Limits and fits governed by DS/EN ISO
286­1:2013, which makes communication with the workshop streamlined and effective
[121].
A free unsupported length of 177 mm is needed for the centrifuge to support both the
vertical and horizontal build plate placement. Appendix A.2 shows a vertical build plate
setup, where the centrifuge can spin 6 Phrozen build plates at a time. Reverse bending
and fatigue are important design aspects to consider if the centrifuge were to be used in an
industrial setting and undergo tens of thousands of revolutions per day. The S­N curve for
steel flattens at its endurance limit, which means that the design should be dimensioned
with a safety factor under this limit [122]. For 304 SS the endurance limit is 240 MPa
[123].
6.5.2
Resin collection
Collection of the removed excess resin for reuse in the printers has been considered to
further increase the efficiency and economic impact of the centrifuge. There are several
considerations to address before reusing resin:
• Contamination ­ All of the printers using the same printer must use the same resin
to avoid cross­contamination. The chamber must also be free from debris and other
contaminants that can compromise the collected resin. The standard of the reused
resin must remain on par with fresh resin.
• UV­exposure ­ The resin should not be exposed to UV from the environment during
its journey from printer to centrifuge and back to the printer. This is achieved through
UV shielding to preserve the photo­initiators in the resin.
• Collection and handling ­ The container used to collect the excess resin should
have a float switch to trigger pickup from the robot arm.
Further studies printing with reused resin as well as chemical analysis of the collected
are needed to determine if the quality meets standards. The company Carbon already
claims to reuse their collected resin, which indicates that it is possible under the right
conditions listed above [58]. Substitute resin removed from the parts during testing was
recovered and reused, proving the concept’s validity. Figure 6.14 shows a cross­section
of the centrifuge, where the slanted false bottom was used to collect resin from the spout
at the lowest point.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
57
==PAGE:70==
Figure 6.14: Section view of MVP centrifuge
6.6
Calculations
Analytical calculations have been used to verify and support the design process. One of
the challenges in the centrifuge build comes from the fact that the shaft is only supported
on one end. The stiffness of the system is crucial to examine, as deformations can create
unwanted stresses and vibrations in the system, as was observed in the earlier prototype.
Some of these load­bearing components have a significant safety factor to avoid mechan­
ical failures during testing and building. Future iterations could be optimized to save on
material cost and manufacturing time.
6.6.1
RCF values for varying radius
The parts experience different centrifugal forces depending on their position on the build
plate. This is mainly due to the default horizontal position, where the entire width of the
build plate greatly influences the perceived radius of the parts as seen in Figure 6.15.
58
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:71==
Figure 6.15: Varying radius depending on build plate position
The difference in radius on the middle of the build plate vs. the edge is calculated in
Equation 6.8. The radius values of 180,76 mm at the edge and 136,5 mm at the middle
are found from the centrifuge assembly in Solidworks.
Radius difference = 1 −(Rmiddle
Redge
) = 1 −( 136, 5mm
180, 76mm) = 24%
(6.8)
Since the radius scales linearly in the RCF formula presented in Equation 6.1, the expe­
rienced relative centrifugal force of a part at the edge of the build plate will be 24% higher
than one in the middle.
6.6.2
Drive Train
A brushless DC motor, 57BLR70­24­01 with hall sensor, was used to drive the centrifuge.
The datasheet for this motor can be seen in Appendix A.16. A BLD­510B motor driver was
paired with an Arduino Mega 2560 to execute the code seen in Appendix A.4.1. There
are multiple ways to control brushless DC motors, but it was decided to use PWM and the
built­in PID controller of the BLD­510B [124]. This resulted in a fluctuation of ± 10 RPM,
which was verified with a Lazmin DT­2234c+ digital tachometer.
A gear ratio of 14 to 30 was used to increase the torque. The maximum speed of the
motor is 3500 RPM which far exceeds the speeds required for this module. Equation 6.9
shows the theoretically maximum RPM of the shaft after gearing.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
59
==PAGE:72==
Maximum RPM after gearing = 3500 RPM · 14
30 ≈1633 RPM
(6.9)
The maximum speed of the motor was found in Appendix A.16. The rated torque is found
here as well and the output is calculated in Equation 6.10.
Torque after gearing = 0, 47 Nm · 30
14 ≈1 Nm
(6.10)
An approximate torque of 1 Nm is calculated without accounting for the losses due to fric­
tion in the belt and pulleys. The following sections use the calculated torque and elaborate
on the absence of friction considerations.
The moment of inertia of the rotating system displayed in Figure 6.13 is found using a
custom coordinate system and the ”Mass Properties”­option in Solidworks. Without prints,
the system is found to have the following moment of inertia:
Izz = 0, 134 kg · m2
(6.11)

It would take approximately 21 seconds to reach 1500 RPM with an angular accelera­
tion of 7.45 rad/s2. These calculations do not account for friction and other losses in the
system and should therefore serve as a guideline. The 57BLR70­24­01 brushless DC
motor is also capable of delivering peak torques exceeding the listed value of 0,47 Nm.
It was observed during testing that the desired speeds could be reached faster than the
calculation shows due to the motor exceeding its rated current of 9 amps.
6.6.3
Decoupling
For the centrifuge build, there has been a focus on decoupling elements and making
components interchangeable to better align with the scaleable and agile vision of the AM
Farm. The following list shows the interchangeable elements of the MVP solution:
• Varying build plate sizes is achievable through the following:
– Universal mounting options
– Interchangeable chamber to allow for varying Z­heights on the build plate
– Size of the flywheel which could allow varying build plate sizes to be mounted.
Bigger build plates would require more space, which a bigger flywheel can
provide.
• RPM of centrifuge can be changed further through the following:
– Motor size
– Gearing
Having built­in flexibility in this module allows it to be compatible with a range of printers
and different print jobs.

speed of 1500 RPM is used based on Equation 6.9. 1500 RPM with a radius of 17 cen­
timetres gives an maximum RCF value of 427 g.

great speeds, where the parts are not expected to stay on the build plate. This kind of
catastrophic failure where either the brim is entirely detached from the build plate or the
part itself is detached from the supports must be avoided, for the machine to be valuable

Equation 6.7 is used to calculate the remaining resin %. The presented value is the
average value from the three build plates, which all were in the machine at the same time.
From the test results, it was clear to see a significant influence of both time and speed on
the cleaning result. This follows the findings from the initial studies, this time with different
geometries of the parts. The low speeds of 200 RPM yielded poor results, which was also
observed with a short spin duration of 20 seconds.

Preliminary centrifuge test results
The results from the preliminary test clearly showed to importance of RPM and time. The
maximum settings of 800 RPM and 60­second spin duration yielded the best outcome,
which could indicate that there is more benefit to be gained from the centrifuge. The cut­
off point for spin duration will be examined for the final round of testing. The benefits
of continued spinning are expected to plateau, but this cut­off value is yet to be found.
It is promising that the MVP centrifuge is removing more than 80% of the applied resin
substitute at its maximum test settings. This is These results once again strengthen the
belief in the pre­cleaning module and the MVP solution.

The RCF values for 1000 and 1200 RPM are 190 g and 273,7 g respectively. The forces

RPM at 9 minutes is chosen as the optimal setting for the centrifuge, based on these
results. With these settings, it is expected to reach the goal of >70% removal of excess
resin, when the system runs with real resin. It is worth noting how lower speeds at 400
RPM for 9 minutes still yield impressive results. These settings might be useful for large
or poorly supported parts.
Further studies on the relationship between RPM/RCF, part weight, and support area %
are needed to provide the best recommendations for the centrifuge settings based on the
print file.

Final recommendation for the system
The MVP centrifuge has proven to yield impressive results throughout various tests with
different benchmark parts. The final recommendations for average­sized parts are found
to be:
• 800 RPM is recommended as a default setting, which will be very effective for most
parts.
• 9­minute spin duration is recommended, as it gives the best results within the takt
time window of 12 minutes, which also allows for the robot arm to move the parts
after spinning.
1200 RPM could be suitable for parts like dental moulds or other lightweight parts with
good adhesion to supports or build plate. 400 RPM is recommended for large and heavy
parts, where spinning still can be effective, but part integrity is the main concern.
6.8
Viability of Pre­Cleaning
This added Pre­Cleaning step introduces additional complexity to the AM Farm process
chain, and its justification is necessary. This section aims to explain the viability of incor­
porating a centrifuge for pre­cleaning parts from financial, operational, and sustainable
perspectives.
The test results indicate that the centrifuge enables more than 80% removal of excess
resin, which can then be recaptured and reused in the printers for new parts. This reduces
the usage of resin in an AM Farm, and significantly improves the solvent’s lifespan before
reaching saturation, thereby aligning with the AM Farm’s sustainability goals.
Equation 6.17 shows the price of resin, assuming that a kernel of 10 printers is using

discount of 25% is applied to the resin price [125].

with a fully automated workflow the printers are expected to print continuously. With an

price of a final centrifuge ready to be implemented in an AM Farm setting, but it would
be estimated that the cost of the machine breaks even within the first year of full­scale
operations with lights­out manufacturing [126].
6.9
Centrifuge ­ Conclusion
The following conclusions for the centrifuge are made based on the results listed in this
chapter:
• Drip time vs. spinning ­ The parts and build plates benefit from dripping, especially
at an angle, after printing to minimize the excess resin stuck to the part and the build
plate. The centrifuge is vastly more effective at removing excess resin in minutes
compared to dripping for up to 24 hours.
• Speed and duration ­ Both RPM and duration greatly influence the performance of
the machine. There is no interaction between these two factors, but higher RPM
and longer spin duration give better performance.
• Efficiency ­ With the optimized parameters found through multiple rounds of testing,
it can be concluded that an efficiency of at least 70% is achievable, which signifi­
cantly reduces the amount of resin needed to be removed during washing.
• Economy ­ A centrifuge built for an AM farm can save thousands of euros per year
in recovered resin and solvent use savings. The machine is expected to break even
within the first year of full­scale production.
It is recommended to implement the centrifuge in an industrial AM Farm based on
these findings.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
69
==PAGE:82==
7
Final Cleaning
This module will start by stating the Final Cleaning requirements, followed by an exper­
imental study of known methods for cleaning resin prints; this is completed to find the
optimal cleaning method along with a solvent. After this, a design, based on these find­
ings of the Final Cleaning module, will be developed and described in detail. Lastly, the
built Final Cleaning module will be characterised through cleaning tests and evaluations
of the benchmark parts.
The Final Cleaning module covers the steps necessary to obtain a 3D printed part free
from excess resin. There are numerous commercial solutions available already as pre­
sented in section 2.2. The aim for this project has been to design and build a cleaning
solution, capable of cleaning all geometries off of resin every time, which easily works in
collaboration with a robot arm to enable a fully automated cleaning step in the process
chain.
7.1
Final Cleaning Requirements
Based on the cleaning module requirements from section 3.3, the specific requirements
for the Final Cleaning module are as follows:
1. The final cleaning module must fully clean a part from residual liquid resin.
2. The final cleaning module shall apply one or more cleaning methods during
the cleaning process.
3. The washing machine should have two vats, one for cleaning and another for
rinsing.
4. The final cleaning module shall perform the cleaning in an automated manner.
5. The washing machine shall have a mount for the universal mounting interface.
6. The cleaning process shall not damage or change the geometry of the printed
parts.
7. The washing machine should incorporate sensors or feedback mechanisms
to trigger maintenance of the components and replacement of solvent.
8. No human operator should come in contact with solvent or resin by the use
of the washing machine.
The above requirements define the scope of the Final Cleaning module; now the following
section will present the experimental study aiming to investigate and determine the best
cleaning method and solvent for removing the residual liquid resin.
7.2
Experimental Cleaning Study
The purpose of the initial cleaning study was to determine which cleaning method and
solvent to use in the final cleaning module presented in this project. There was already a
protocol for cleaning and rinsing 3D printed parts in the institute lab, and that method will
be used to compare the performance of the other methods being tested.
70
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:83==
7.2.1
Experimental Design
This section will present the experimental design of the cleaning study and the rationales
hereof.
There are several factors that can affect the results, thus the experimental design is crit­
ical to extract truthful information. Firstly, the methods and a quantifiable performance
metric need to be established. For these experiments, the mass of the 3D printed parts,
before and after cleaning, was chosen because an increase in mass would likely be due
to residual resin when solvent absorption is taken into consideration. Visual assessment
in LOM is used to assess the cleanliness of surfaces. Secondly, the benchmark part
called Top part, which was presented in section 4.4, was selected for this study because
it is an example of a real commercial part with small features. The benchmark part for
the cleaning study was used to compare the performance of the different methods and
solvents. Lastly, the methods and solvents themselves need to be defined. The following
methods and solvents were chosen based on the State of the Art in section 2.2, available
equipment, and allowed solvents in DTU’s VPP lab.
Cleaning Methods
• Solvent soak (static cleaning): Was chosen as a base cleaning method, as there is
neither movement of the solvent nor parts, only diffusional dissolution of the residual
resin.
• Stirring (parts are stationary, the solvent is magnetically stirred): Magnetic stirring
was chosen as it is used in many commercial products for cleaning VPP 3D printed
parts.
• Ultrasonication: Was chosen because it is part of the standard protocol, and known
the ability to clean very small and intricate items or parts with complex shapes
• Swirling (parts are swirled around in static solvent): Was chosen due to the hypoth­
esis that it could be more efficient than stirring as it was assumed that there would
be stronger solvent currents and/or more turbulence around a part moving through
the solvent, than solvent spinning around a part.
• Ultrasonication and swirling: Was chosen to test if the informed assessment of
ultrasonication and swirling would perform better than the other methods.
Solvents
• Isopronol (IPA): It was chosen because it is the industry standard when cleaning
parts made using SLA and thus will be considered as the benchmark solvent [127,
128, 129, 130, 131].
• Tripropylene Glycol Monomethyl Ether (TPM): It was chosen because it is a safer
alternative to IPA, and it can absorb roughly 3 times as much resin [132, 133] com­
pared to IPA, meaning it need not be replaced as often as IPA does.
• Anmasi SLA Cleaner 2000: It was also chosen because it is a safer alternative to
IPA and for its cleaning efficiency data and low corrosion of parts. [134, 42].
Both TPM and Anmasi prescribe a final rinse­off of the solvent in deionized water.

After having established the benchmark part, cleaning methods, and solvents being tested,
the following section will describe the setup of the experiments and the prerequisite infor­
mation needed. Table 7.1 describes the series of tests and their settings, aimed to gather

To prepare for the test regiment, 15 Top parts were printed at a 50% scale to fit 5 parts
per print on an Elegoo Mars 4 DLP 3D printer [135] using Prima Creator Solid Black resin
[136]. They were printed with the support structure and raft setup by the Lychee Slicer
[137]. The part orientation, support structure, and raft are shown in Figure 7.1.
Figure 7.1: Top part with support and raft for printing.
All parts were subjected to the standard cleaning procedure described in section 2.2.4
and were UV­cured at 60 °C for 45 min, such that a reference mass could be established
and the parts re­used for the various tests. The reference mass for each part was used
as the ”grand truth” for which any increase in mass would be compared. This means that
any increase in mass would be assumed to originate from residual resin when corrected
for soaking. To calculate a change in mass, the following formula was used:
Current mass −Reference mass = ∆mass[g]
(7.1)
72
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:85==
Using formula 7.1, a positive value means that there is residual resin on the part, and a
negative value means that the cleaned part is ”more” clean than the reference (washed
using the standard procedure). The parts were engraved with an identifying number, to
enable tracking of the consistency of each operation of the tests, like coating and cleaning
(re­setting) consistency. Swirling tests and the combination of ultrasonication and swirling
were not conducted in TPM and Anmasi due to changes to lab­work permissions. The
total number of runs was calculated using the following formula:
Number of runs = # of different tests
·(# of factors · # of samples)
−(# of dropped runs due to changes)
= 5 · (3 · 5) −(4 · 5) = 55 runs
(7.2)
7.2.3
Solvent Soak Test
Before any of the cleaning methods were tested, solvent soak tests were performed to
evaluate how much of an increase in the mass of parts could be attributed to soaking of
the solvent into the part, seeing that filament printers, using thermopolymers, are hydro­
scopic, while VPP printers typically use thermoset polymers, can also exhibit hydroscopic
behavior [138][139]. Before the test began, a solution with a 50/50 % [volume] ratio of
Anmasi and deionized water was prepared and mixed for 1 hour using a magnetic stirrer
at 800 rpm. Furthermore, parts were weighed using a Mettler Toledo Precision Balance
ML203T/00, with a precision of ±0.7 mg [140]. The parts were soaked in their respective
solvent for 30 min, which is double the amount of time the parts are exposed to solvent
compared to one round of the standard cleaning procedure (15 min total). Figure 7.2
shows what the test setup looked like. Figures 7.2b to 7.2d illustrate the condition of the
parts after the test. Notice how the difference in surface finish: IPA is matte, while TPM
seemed waxy and reflective, and Anmasi was slightly reflective.
After the test was completed, the parts were weighed again to measure the difference in
mass.

value is R2=0.95 for IPA, R2=0.998 for TPM, and R2=0.97 for Anmasi, indicating a strong
fit for the regression model.

3. The parts are subjected to the cleaning method described in section 7.2.2, table 7.1.
4. The parts are weighed on the precision balance scale.
5. The parts are cleaned (reset) using the standard cleaning procedure.

==PAGE:90==
The ”Methods” category has a low p­value, instilling confidence that the results are truthful
if it is concluded that there is at least one of the tested methods that is significantly differ­
ent from the others. The power level is 79% just below the threshold of 80% which would
indicate a slightly higher probability that the choice of believing that there is a significant
difference between the means, which would be a Type II error. With such a low p­value, it
was concluded that there is a significant difference, as an increase in sample size would
increase the power level above the threshold. The ANOVA assumptions were verified.
The ”Solvent” category has a very high p­value, strongly indicating that there is no signif­
icant evidence to conclude that there is a significant difference between the means. The
power level is very low; 6.5% meaning that there is a 93.5 % chance of making a wrong
decision if the conclusion is that there is a significant difference between the means.
Figure 7.7 are box plots that illustrate the ANOVA results graphically. It can be observed
from figure 7.7b that there seems to be no significant difference between the means of
the ”Solvents” category, which was also concluded from the p­value and power level.
The ANOVA results from the ”Methods” category indicated strong evidence that there is
a significant difference between at least one of the means. In order to discern specifi­
cally which ones are different from each other, the post­hoc method, Tukey’s HSD test is
employed, which was described in section 4.5.

DKK/l (20 l container) [142] and IPA costs 64 DKK/l (25 l container ­ DTU price at Sigma­

kr/l. Additionally, Anmasi can dissolve ≈3 times as much as IPA, making the effective
price 17.8 DKK/l. TPM can also dissolve 3 times more resin compared to IPA, making
the effective price 51.1 DKK/l compared to IPA. Anmasi was subjectively more pleasant
to work with. TPM left a very waxy finish, which could be difficult to rinse off while Anmasi
was easily rinsed with water. Both TPM and Anmasi are less hazardous than IPA and do
not have ATEX regulations as strict as the ones for IPA storage volumes [36, 37, 41, 39].
7.3
Conclusion of Cleaning Study
With the results presented, it can be concluded that:
• After 30 min the 3D printed parts soak solvent and increased in mass by: 0.082%,
2.22% and 1.94% for IPA, TPM and Anmasi respectively
• The evaporation rate was approximated with an exponential regression and illus­
trated the volatile nature of IPA.
80
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:93==
• The resin coating method was consistent: 0.627 ± 0.023 g resin to each part. Used
as correction for measured mass increase for the methods of testing
• There was a significant difference between the means of the methods
• The chosen cleaning method was the combination of ultrasonication and swirling,
even though it is not significantly different from its constituents alone, but different
from magnetic stirring and static cleaning. The constituents were not different from
stirring alone, thus the choice became the combination.
• The tested solvents did not show any significant difference in their ability to clean
3D printed parts. Anmasi was chosen due to its cheaper effective price at 17.8 kr/l
and subjectively more pleasant to use
7.4
Washing Machine Design
The following section outlines the design of the automated washing machine. This design
incorporates findings from a cleaning study, which identified the most effective methods
and solvents for cleaning resin parts. Firstly, the specific requirements for a washing ma­
chine are stated, and then the development and selection of components for the washing
machine follows.
7.4.1
Washing Machine Requirements
Based on system requirements concerning automation, AM Farm integration, and the
cleaning study that determined the most effective cleaning methods, the requirements for
the washing machine are stated as the following:
1. The washing machine must fully clean the part from uncured resin.
2. The washing machine shall apply both ultrasonication and swirling during the clean­
ing process.
3. The washing machine must have two vats, one for cleaning and another for rinsing.
4. The washing machine shall have automated submerging and raising of the build
plate.
5. The washing machine shall have a mount for the universal mounting interface.
6. The cleaning process must effectively remove uncured resin without damaging or
changing the geometry of the printed parts.
7. The washing machine should incorporate sensors or feedback mechanisms to trig­
ger maintenance of the components and replacement of solvent.
8. No human operator should come in contact with solvent or resin by the use of the
washing machine
With the washing machine requirements established, the design of the washing machine
can follow.
7.4.2
Selection of Ultrasonication
The complexity of developing ultrasonic equipment stems from the necessity to inte­
grate multiple transducers with the main chamber, ensuring compatibility and functionality
across the entire system. In the context of the project, this added a significant layer of
complexity. Extensive research was conducted on various suppliers and solutions, but all
options adhered to an Engineer­to­Order process, which inevitably led to high lead times
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
81
==PAGE:94==
and costs [144, 145, 146, 147, 55, 148]. Ultimately, in the interest of time, cost, and sim­
plicity, it was decided to purchase two off­the­shelf 30­liter ultrasonic cleaners as seen in
Figure 7.8.
Figure 7.8: VEVOR 30 liter ultrasonic cleaner ­ From [149]
The VEVOR ultrasonic cleaner operates at a frequency of 40 kHz and has an ultrasonic
power of 600 W, accompanied by heating elements rated at 500 W [149]. This setup
allows temperature control of solvents in the cleaning vats. The vat used for the exper­
imental testing (Elmasonic P 30H [150]) was set to 50% power, which yields a power
density, assuming operation at their respective recommended fill capacity, of:
Elmasonic P 30H :
100[W]
1.9[l]
· 50% = 26.3 W/l
Compared to VEVOR 30l:
VEVOR 30l :
600[W]
20[l]
= 30 W/l
The similarity between the power pr. liter was a consideration when choosing the VEVOR
vat, in conjunction with its physical size (20 cm vat depth). The frequency of the ultrasonic
cleaner used for experimental testing was set at 80 kHz, with higher frequencies known
for their effectiveness in cleaning smaller features [151]. However, the ultrasonic cleaner
purchased for the washing machine operates at 40 kHz, which may be less effective at
cleaning fine features in printed parts compared to the 80 kHz unit used in the experi­
ments. Nevertheless, vats of the necessary capacity for the swirling of build plates were
82
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:95==
not commercially available at frequencies higher than 40 kHz.
Having two separate ultrasonic units made it possible to compartmentalise the required
motion system for cleaning and rinsing with the washing. This made it possible to develop
one solution and then duplicate it before building everything into a centralised solution in
the frame.
7.4.3
Motion system
In order to automate the final cleaning process and ensure higher efficiency and consis­
tency, a motion system needed to be incorporated. The choice of ultrasonic cleaners
creates a natural design envelope (20 cm vat depth) for integration of the motion sys­
tem. The motion system is necessary for automating cleaning consisting of the following
elements:
First, a Z­stage mechanism is used to submerge the build plates into the cleaning solvent
and then lift them out upon completion of the cleaning cycle. Followed by the requirement
of swirling the parts, the motion system includes a rotation unit. This unit is responsible for
creating a swirling motion of the parts on the build plate in the solvent, thereby improving
the overall cleaning efficacy.
Since the mechanical system in the final cleaning module does not experience high stresses
during operation, it was decided to build the motion system using components from an old
Ultimaker 2+ [152]. The availability of the decommissioned Ultimaker 2+ machines, from
DTU’s Fablab significantly reduced the time required to obtain components and the over­
all cost of the motion system. The Ultimakers were disassembled and the components
needed for the motion system were extracted. For the Z­stage of the washing machine
design, the axis rods and Z­screw, coupled with the stepper motor, were utilized for the
vertical movement. It had previously been confirmed with a small capacity test, that the
Z­stage was able to transport loads of up to at least 3.5 kg without stalling. A regular sce­
nario for the washing machine would be around 1.25 kg, including the weight of the build
plate, mount, and resin­printed parts. The aluminium plate, originally utilised to connect
the heated build plate to the Z­stage of the Ultimaker 2+, was repurposed and modified
to accommodate the rotational unit and the universal coupling mechanism described in
Section 5. The Z­stage was mounted on a 40x40 aluminium extrusion frame, using two
brackets made of bend sheet metal; drawings can be found in Appendix A.14.
The rotation unit is built using a stepper motor from the Ultimaker 2+’s CoreXY motion
system. A 5 mm motor shaft connects to an 8 mm shaft using an Oldham coupling to
manage parallel misalignment [153]. The 8 mm shaft came from an M8 bolt with the head
removed, onto which the universal mounting interface is assembled for mounting build
plates. To prevent disassembly due to alternating rotations, a set screw is screwed into
the side of the M8 bolt to secure it in place.The entire motion system with the Z­stage and
rotation unit, assembled on the aluminium profile structure, can be seen in Figure 7.9.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
83
==PAGE:96==
Figure 7.9: Motion system on the Washing Machine
7.4.4
Electronics
In order to control the motion system, a Raspberry Pi 4 was used as the main control
unit, which controlled two Arduino Mega 2560 [154] microcontrollers. Each Arduino was
connected to two DM332T digital stepper drivers [155], which controlled the motors for
the z stage and the rotation unit, respectively. One contact switch for each of the two
Arduino’s was connected in a ”normally open” configuration and used to enable homing
of the Z­stage. A simple schematic diagram that illustrates the electrical system of the
washing machine can be seen in Figure 7.10.
84
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:97==
Figure 7.10: Circuit diagram of the washing machine system
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
85
==PAGE:98==
In order to automate the ultrasonic vats, they were disassembled to access the controller
unit on them. The positive and ground terminals on each push­button on the controller
board were connected to a prototype circuit board with six NPN BC547 transistors, one
for each push­button. Ground was connected to the emitter, 5V was connected to the
collector, and a digital pin from the Arduino Mega was connected to the base with a 300
ohm current limiting resistor. This was done in order to short the buttons electronically
from the Arduinos and control the ultrasonic vats. This circuit board can be seen at the
bottom of Figure 7.10, the resistors are hidden on the backside of the boards.
Figure 7.11 depicts how the wiring and ”hacking” of the VEVOR ultrasonic vat was done.
