7.4.5
Code
A touchscreen connected to the Raspberry Pi was used to control the washing machine
via a user interface, which can be seen in Figure 7.12. The code for the Raspberry Pi can
be found in Appendix A.4.3. The Pi had a serial connection to the two Arduino’s, which
handled motion control and control of the ultrasonic vats. A simple algorithm needed
to be made to control the timings and temperatures of the ultrasonic cleaners. So, the
Arduino knew the default settings of the vat and how the controller on the vat acted during
operation, regarding timer resetting, etc. The code has rotation speed in RPM, time, and
heat settings as primary inputs. Through serial input, the parameters can be set, and
cleaning cycles can be started and stopped. When initiating a cleaning cycle, the build
plate is lowered into the vat, starts ultrasonication, and starts rotating with the set RPM.
The direction of rotation alternates every quarter of the total duration set for the cleaning
cycle. The Arduino code for controlling washing machine units can be found in Appendix
A.4.2 and the over­all structure of the code can be seen in figure 7.13.

7.4.6
Structual frame
The structural frame for the washing machine was mainly constructed form 40x40 alu­
minium extrusions and some bent sheet metal parts, for mounting the motion system to
the frame. The skeleton, without electronics and enclosure, can be seen in Figure 7.14.
Figure 7.14: The aluminium extrusion skeleton for the washing machine
Enclosure and sound damping
When the Ultrasonic cleaner from Vevor was first tested, a noise level above 105 dB
was measured. Ultrasonic cleaners that operate at 40 kHz tend to generate more noise
compared to those operating at higher frequencies, since 40 kHz is closer to the range of
frequencies perceptible by the human ear [151]. Therefore, a sound damping enclosure
was needed to lower the noise level of the vats. It consisted of noise damping foam and
plywood. Acrylic sheets were used on the top of the washing machine to ensure it could
handle liquid spills. Additionally, two lids were made from acrylic sheets to close of the
vat from the top. This effectively lower the noise level from 105 dB to 75 dB. The washing
machine with the sound damping enclosure is shown in Figure 7.15.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
89
==PAGE:102==
Figure 7.15: The plywood and sound­foam effectively dampened the sound

7.5.2
First Round of DoE
For the DoE, Fourth iteration Benchmark parts were chosen as they were designed with
centrifuging tests in mind, such that the parts could be washed in the washing machine
and then be part of the centrifuge tests. they were printed using Phrozen Mighty 8k and
unpigmented resin from FTD. The build plates were carefully cleaned by hand to establish
a somewhat ”clean” mass. The build plates were weighed and subsequently covered in
resin using a syringe, spraying into every hole and crevise. The helixes were filled from
the bottom through a fill­hole and had a screw inserted to make the feature a blind helix.
After 4 runs it was gathered that the 4th iteration of the benchmark part was not well suited
for these tests: The support structures broke, the blind helix was not cleaned at all, and
spilled excess resin onto the rest of the relatively clean structures, making it difficult to
determine the efficacy of the treatment. Figure 7.17 illustrates how the typical benchmark
parts would end up looking after being cleaned in the washing machine. The part would
be relatively clean, but became contaminated with resin from the blind helix as it would
leak and spill from the hole.

7.5.3
Ultrasonication Endurance Test
Having experienced that unpigmented resin did not endure ultrasonication well after only
a cumulative time of 20 min. It was deemed necessary to perform an ultrasonication
endurance test to see how the FTD black resin would be affected by ultrasonic treatment
at 40kHz. The test was conducted by firstly printing a batch of ”top” benchmark parts
using Phrozen Mighty 4k and subjecting the entire BP to ultrasonication. One part was
retrieved from the BP before the test started and gently swirled in Anmasi for 1 min, then
rinsed in water.
Figure 7.19 displays the series of pictures where the test duration was 50 minutes, only
punctuated by retrieving a part every 5 minutes. Figure 7.19f looks particularly bad, but
this is unfortunately due to an unlucky retrieval of a part with print defects stemming from

the surface striation becomes increasingly prominent. Compared to the unpigmented
resin, the surface and all small geometries as a whole fared much better with the black
resin from FTD.

7.5.4
Second Round of DoE
After having established ultrasonic cleaning effects on the unpigmented resin, it was de­

used previously in this project for characterizing the performance of cleaning.
The test regiment is the same as presented in table 7.6. An example of a build plate can
be seen in figure 7.20 which illustrates a typical test specimen. Notice the gradual degra­
dation of part surface quality towards the back. This behavior is discussed in Section
4.3.1. As a precaution, the ”Top” were removed from their support structures, because
any misalignment in the build plate when calibrating the Z­axis of the printers, could have
resulted in uneven heights on the parts. The parts were scribed for tracking and measured
using the Mettler precision balance and there was a strong correlation between position
and surface quality. The findings can be seen in Figure 7.21. It should be noted that the
numbers on the figure are part mass without support structures.
Due to this correlation, it was not possible to treat the data as ”one build plate pr. treat­
ment”, it was necessary to track all parts individually after each treatment and thus make
a reference comparison which was location­based.
7.5.5
Results
In the following section, the results JMP analysis from the washing machine characteri­
zation DoE will be discussed. The complete JMP analysis can be seen in appendix A.4.3
where it is shown that the ANOVA assumptions were verified.
From figure 7.22 it can be observed that temperature, time, interaction between time and
swirl speed, and interaction between time and temperature are significant factors as their
p­values are below the threshold of α=0.05, meaning the null­hypothesis can be rejected
for these factors. Interestingly, swirling speed is insignificant agreeing with the findings
from section 7.2 indicating that swirling was not significantly different from the combination
of ultrasonication and swirling.

Figure 7.22: Effect Summary
The relative total importance of all the significant factors can be seen on figure 7.23.
Temperature contributes 77.4 % of the total effect while time and speed contribute 24.2%
and 6% respectively. This indicates that the cleaning efficacy of the washing machine is
highly dependent on the solvent temperature and swirling speed has almost no effect.
Figure 7.23: Variable importance

• Swirl speed vs temperature: No interaction
• Swirl speed vs time: Interaction Detected
• Time vs temperature: weak interaction
This means that swirl speed and time interact and have a significant effect on the results,
even though swirl speed was not a significant factor. There is also a weak interaction
effect between time and temperature.

the reference part.

7.5.6
Short extra test
After analyzing the results, test 8 settings (4 min, 60 rpm, 60 °C) were concluded to have
performed the best in terms of removing resin/mass relative to its reference part. Three
build plates of the 8th iteration of benchmark parts were printed and cleaned using these
settings. The thin wall geometries, as can be seen in Figure 4.8 were missing on several
build plates indicating that, in conjunction with the findings from JMP, swirling speed is an
insignificant factor and it will damage fragile geometric features. Due to this finding, it was
determined that the second best­performing settings were to be used: Test 6 with 4 min,
20 rpm, and 60 °C.
7.6
Final Cleaning ­ Conclusion
• The washing machine did not wash the blind helix hole successfully.
• The 4th iteration Benchmark part was not well suited for cleaning and subsequent
centrifugation tests.
• Printing forces affected the surface quality necessitating a more structured data col­
lection
• Temperature, time, and the interaction between time and swirl speed were the most
significant factors of the washing machine
• Temperature was by far the most important variable in terms of its effect on the
performance (77.4%). The second most important variable was time at 24.2 %
• There was a significant interaction between time and swirl speed.
• The most desirable settings were: Increased temperature and time. But there is a
limit to how warm the solvent can heat before going outside of normal operating
• Significant crack formation formed the longer and warmer a treatment lasted. The
reference parts were also damaged by ultrasonication in Elmasonics 80kHz.
• Black resin from Fun To Do handles ultrasonication significantly better albeit surface
defects and cracks formed gradually over a treatment time of up to 50 min.
• Un­pigmented resin from FTD does not handle ultrasonic cleaning well, and higher
frequencies and lower energy­dense cleaners should be explored.
• The best­looking parts were cleaned using 2 min, 20 rpm at 25 °C, but the cleanest
parts were cleaned using 4 min, 60 rpm, 60 °C
• Very fragile and thin features did not survive treatment with settings of 4 min, 60
rpm, 60 °C, so the next best­performing settings were 4 min, 20 rpm, 60 °C
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
99
==PAGE:112==
8
Cleaning System Validation
In the following chapter, the centrifuge and washing machine system will be evaluated
together. The combination of these two systems describes the Cleaning System which
was developed to satisfy the requirements previously set in Section 3.3.
Firstly, the timing schedule of the cleaning module in combination with the reminder of
the AM Farm steps is analysed to validate that the cleaning module will not be a bottle­
neck in the AM Farm. This is followed by a cleaning system performance test, where the
combination of the centrifuge and washing machine process will be evaluated up against
manually cleaning.
The resulting post­processing chain, when implementing the centrifuge and washing ma­
chine is illustrated in Figure 8.1. It has timing estimates included. The time estimate
for support removal cannot be verified since further research needs to be done in this
area. However if the support removal were to be done manually it is estimated to take
approximately 10 min.
Figure 8.1: Proposed Post­processing chain, with timings for each step.
8.1
Timing Schedule
For validating that the cleaning module is not a bottleneck in the post­processing chain,
the processing times must be adjusted within the takt time and the subsequent process
steps must be mapped to the results from testing the modules individually showed that
the recommended centrifuge time was 9 min and washing time was 4 min. The ”effec­
tive time” per build plate in the centrifuge is 3 min, since there are 3 build plates being
post­processed simultaneously. This results in a tact­time per build plate of 7 min which
is within the proposed takt time of 12 min per build plate. With the post­process time
established, an example of an AM Farm setup with 10 printers in a kernel using the opti­
mized parameters can be seen in Figure 8.2. This figure attempts to show the worst­case
scenario where 10 printers have finished at the same time. Three build plates are loaded
in the centrifuge at a time, and the next spinning cycle can only start when the final build
100
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:113==
plate is moved from the centrifuge to the washing station. Handling of the build plates by
the robot arm between each operation is included in the displayed operation time. Figure
8.3 illustrates how the printing time is the biggest bottleneck with the current setup. It is
divided into two sections, hazardous and operator­safe, to highlight how UV­curing marks
the point where it is no longer possible to come in contact with uncured resin. Both of
these figures aim to highlight how the post­processing system proposed in this project is
able to handle peak loads and still maintain a healthy buffer.
Figure 8.3 shows the complete journey from the finished print to the clean and final part.
8.1.1
Build Plates Required
The 100­minute minimum build plate occupation seen in figure 8.3 is found from the mo­
ment the build plate enters the post­processing system after dripping. The following cal­
culation shows the number of build plates needed to sustain a kernel running continuously
at maximum capacity as shown in figure 8.2.
BP’s = Drip time + Post­processing + Print time
Print time
= 6 + 100 + 120
120
= 1.88 ≈2
(8.1)
Two build plates per printer should theoretically be enough based on equation 8.1. It is
important that the build plate is returned to the correct printer to ensure proper levelling.
Having three build plates per printer would introduce additional contingency. Running
the AM Farm and collecting information on maintenance and down time would provide a
better basis for recommending the correct amount of build plates for a kernel.
8.1.2
Curing
For curing, it is shown how 4 UV­curing units with a curing time of up to 60 minutes is
enough to service a kernel. Here, the entire build plate with prints is loaded in the curing
station. 60 minutes for curing is used as it covers the majority of resins used in VPP [156].
Having individual curing stations also allows for customized curing times depending on
geometries. Tracking of the build plates from printer to curing would be necessary for this
to work. Such tracking is well implemented in the industry and could be done in numerous
ways such as QR codes or [157]. Having individual curing units also follows the goal of a
low­maintenance system where the machines can be swapped out if needed.
8.2
Cleaning System Performance Testing
In order to evaluate the cleaning systems performance against a manual cleaning pro­
cess, two full run with centrifuging and automated washing with three build plates were
performed with the settings in Table 8.1. Two additional build plates were printed and
completely manually cleaned as a benchmark. The prints on the build plate with the sev­
enth iteration benchmark part and the gyroid structure took 2 hours to print, which results
in a total of 16 hours of print time for the final system tests, as the parts needed to be
fresh and unprocessed for validating the cleaning system.

8.2.1
System Testing Preparations
For the system tests, it was decided to use actual resin from build plates coming straight
from the printer. This was done in an effort to mimic the industrial setting of the proposed
AM Farm. The latest iteration of the benchmark part as seen in Figure 4.8, and a gyroid
structure was printed with unpigmented FTD industrial resin on the Phrozen printers [125]
[87]. The build plates were put on hold to drip, while the other prints were finalized. This
meant that all build plates got at least 10 minutes of drip time, while some were dripping
for hours. The build plates were all weighed along the cleaning process and the parts
were weighted individually after drying and build plate removal.
Safety
While performing these tests safety requirements were implemented as real resin was
being used for the tests:
• Location ­ All tests with real resin were conducted in the VPP lab, where all of the
facilities for chemical waste disposal and ventilation are present.
• Equipment ­ Lab coats and nitrile gloves were worn at all times as well as goggles
and hearing protection, which has also been used for previous tests.
• Orientation ­ All present personnel were briefed ahead of testing and the proper
personal protection equipment was offered.
• Emergency kit ­ The location of emergency equipment was well­known ahead of
testing.
• Contingency planning ­ Clear plans to deal with dangerous spills and mechanical
failures.
• Waste disposal ­ Prepared containers with correct labeling were present to properly
dispose of the chemicals after use.
The tests were also authorized by the relevant DTU staff. All of these preparations resulted
in successful and safe tests, which can be replicated by others if needed.
As both Phrozen printers were used it was important to track whether the build plate
came from Minty or Sassy. The differences in light intensity between the printers has
influence on the mass of the final components as seen in Table 4.2. This difference was
compensated for when analysing the data.
The two build plates used for benchmark testing were cleaned using the standard protocol.
The parts were removed from the build plate before cleaning since the parts themselves
are the only areas of interest. Each build plate contains two benchmark parts and two
gyroid parts as seen in figure 8.4.

serves as a milestone in the feasibility of the AM Farm.
8.2.2
Qualitative Cleaning Analysis
Additional LOM analysis was used to verify the claim of being albe to automate the clean­
ing process for an AM farm. LOM analysis helped to confirm the cleanliness of the parts,
and investigate corrosion of surfaces/cracks. The Zeiss Stemi 2000­C was used for this
purpose [141]. The software DeltaPix was used to capture the images [158]. A calibration
disc was used to ensure correct measurements at different magnifications.
The following side­by­side LOM pictures aim to highlight the difference between the man­
ual and automatic cleaning methods. All of the pictures have manual (left) and automatic
(right). The original measurements from CAD are found in Appendix A.1.
Figure 8.6: Remaining resin in 12 mm deep blind holes with varying diameters
Figure 8.6 shows how automatic cleaning is significantly more efficient in cleaning the
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
105
==PAGE:118==
vertical blind holes of the benchmark part. Only the 0.5 mm diameter hole and the very
bottom of the 1 mm hole have resin left, whereas the manually cleaned part has a lot left.
It is very difficult to use compressed air to clean blind holes, where the centrifuge seems
to be more effective. It was observed that the centrifugal force mainly had its benefits in
the direct line of sight, perpendicular to the build plate.
Figure 8.7: Top view of blind holes with measured diameters
Figure 8.7 shows slight differences in the surface quality, but both of them have the same
matte surface which is expected from a clean part.
Figure 8.8: Pillars from the ”forest” on top of the bench measured
Figure 8.8 shows the pillars in the small forest on top of the Benchmark part. A clear differ­
ence is visible here, where multiple cracks are present on the automatically cleaned part.
This is likely due to the ultrasonication from the 40 kHz VEVOR ultrasonic cleaners, which
has a slightly higher power density than the ultrasonic chamber used in manual cleaning.
The difference in frequencies, with 40 kHz in the vats for the automatic Washing Machine
and 80 kHz from manually performed cleaning, as well as the increased temperature in
106
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:119==
the automatic system can damage the surfaces of parts and introduce cracks, through
the higher intensity of cavitation [5, 31]. This observation implies that the specifications
and settings of the VEVOR ultrasonic cleaners are too powerful for fine features.
Figure 8.9: Ribs with different levels of cracks
Figure 8.9 shows how the dimensions of the thin­walled features are not significantly
changed due to centrifuging, this is confirming the centrifugal forces does not pull the
features of the Benchmark part out of shape. Cracks are however present in the images,
which is likely due to the aforementioned factors.

8.3
Cleaning System Conclusion
The following conclusions for the cleaning system are made based on the results listed in
this chapter:
• The cleaning system met the requirement of fully cleaning complex parts from un­
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
107
==PAGE:120==
cured resin. Although narrow blind holes proved difficult, they were cleaning better
then by manual means.
• The printed parts successfully maintained the adhesion of printed parts and supports
to the build plate throughout the cleaning process. With the settings dialled in on
the centrifuge at 800 RPM at 9 min, none of the printed part flew off.
• The centrifuge and washing machine, operates within the takt time of 12 min, en­
suring it is not a bottleneck in the post­processing chain. It was validated that the
centrifuge and washing machine could support at least 10 VPP printers.
• The cleaning system recycles a significant amount of uncured resin >70% , con­
tributing to sustainability and cost­effectiveness in an AM farm.
• The cleaning process effectively removes residual resin without altering the geom­
etry of the printed parts. However, surface finish of the parts cleaned with the auto­
mated system is not optimal. This might be due to the resin choice in combination
with the settings of the ultrasonic cleaners. These factors can be changed and fine­
tuned during future testing of the automated post­processing system.
• The automated cleaning system eliminates human interaction with hazardous resin
and solvents, ensuring operator safety post­processing phases. The goal of creating
an automated MVP system for cleaning resin prints was realised.
These conclusions indicate that the cleaning system fulfills the critical requirements out­
lined in Section 3.3 of the thesis, contributing to a safe, efficient, and effective post­
processing solution in the AM Farm
The goal of realising an automated MVP system capable of capturing excess resin
and eliminating the need for human contact during the hazardous phases of post­
processing has been realised. The system serves as a milestone in the realization
of the AM Farm.

9
Discussion
The work in this project has covered a broad area within VPP post­processing, machine
build, and testing. Some of the considerations and findings made in that process will be
presented and discussed in the following chapter.
9.1
Pre­Cleaning Module
This section pertains to discussing the pre­cleaning module which is the centrifuge.
9.1.1
Part deformation
There have been concerns from multiple parties regarding the deformation of the parts
while spinning in the centrifuge. The parts are yet to be cured and achieve their final
mechanical properties, which could make them vulnerable to deformation.
All of the tests as well as the LOM pictures did not show any sign of deformation after spin­
ning in the centrifuge. The designated areas, thin walls, of the benchmark parts for testing
deformation remained in their original state. It is possible that parts bridging significant
distances could deform. Figure 9.1 shows a proposed set­up where an added mass can
be used to check deformation after spinning. This could be achieved by printing threads
and then adding different lengths of bolts to vary the mass.
Figure 9.1: Deformation of part with added mass due to spinning
A dedicated study of this effect could improve understanding of the effects that the cen­
trifuge in a VPP setting has and avoid potential damage to parts in the AM Farm.
9.2
Final Cleaning Module
This section pertains to discussing the final cleaning module which is the washing ma­
chine.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
111
==PAGE:124==
9.2.1
Ultrasonic cleaning
The use of ultrasonic cleaning showed promising results during the initial study of cleaning
methods. The machine used in the lab emitted 80 kHz waves, while the 30­liter ultrasonic
chamber bought for the MVP solution emitted 40 kHz waves. This difference in wave­
length is likely to have caused some of the issues seen during testing, where cracks were
present. A dialog with the company SinapTec explored the possibility of a custom­made
ultrasonic solution [144]. This was possible but vastly more expensive and with longer
lead time compared to the off­the­shelf part that was acquired.
Having a solution where the power and frequency can be varied would set the premise
for a deeper study of the effect of the dependency between feature size and ultrasonic
frequency.
9.2.2
Washing Machine design
The design of the washing machine implemented two ultrasonic cleaners to allow clean­
ing with various solvents such as Anmasi and TPM, grouped as glycol ethers, which are
solvents that need a rinse step in water to remove the residual solvent. This means that
ultrasonication of the parts happens both in the vat with the solvent and in the vat with the
deionized water. Early on in the design and development of the washing machine, it was
decided to have two ultrasonic cleaners instead of just one, to effectively rinse the solvent
off and increase the versatility of the system. However, during testing with Anmasi, which
is made from Diethylene Glycol Monobutyl Ether, it was found that ultrasonication for rins­
ing off solvent was not necessary, as a gentle swirl was sufficient which could abscond the
VPP printed parts from unnecessary exposure to ultrasonication. The washing machine
could then, in turn, have an Anmasi cleaning solution in both of the vats to enable double
throughput. Where the rinse step in deionised water could be done in a simple container,
swiftly by the robot arm in the AM Farm kernel, before moving on to the drying module.
The washing machine could also implement the more common solvent IPA, safety mea­
sures for complying with ATEX T6 classification, as well as features to limit IPA from
evaporating. With IPA as the cleaning solvent, the first vat could be responsible for the
coarse wash and the second vat could be responsible for the fine wash. This could be
beneficial since studies have shown that resin­contaminated IPA can be recycled [159],
whereas glycol ether­based solvents are yet to be researched for its recyclability after
contamination with photopolymers.
9.3
Cleaning System
9.3.1
Scalability
The proposed MVP solutions are meant to be part of the proposed AM Farm. The auto­
mated farm aims to compete with classic injection moulding on agility, cost, and lead time
(seen in Appendix A.4.3). The scalability of such a farm is important to accommodate
large orders from multiple customers. Here, it is proposed that kernels of approximately
10 printers are connected to their post­processing stations. It is beneficial to only print
with one type of resin per kernel to avoid cross­contamination in the centrifuge. Remov­
ing the excess resin from parts with different resins will still improve the solvent use, but
it will hinder the collection and re­use of the removed resin.
Scaleability is achievable with the proposed machines through the amount of spinning,
washing, drying, and curing stations. The physical floor space in each kernel might be a
limiting factor. Combining the small post­processing units with larger single units might
help combat this issue. For now, it is however prioritised that small and interchangeable
112
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:125==
units are used to make the AM Farm a reality. Contentious improvements on the ma­
chines, layout, process settings, and resins will allow further optimization and scalability
of the automated post­processing.
9.3.2
Post­processing Order
The proposed post­processing order Figure 8.1, has post­curing, prior to the removal of
the support structure. This is scheduled in this sequence to make the support removal by
an operator a safe process where PPE is unnecessary. However, if the support removal
process were to be completely automated, this step could occur immediately after drying.
The automation of support removal could come from a cobot arm programmed to per­
form the movement to tear the supports off, from the specific component. Alternatively,
methods from the injection moulding industry could be investigated for their applicability
in support structure removal, such as ultrasonic de­gating [160].
9.3.3
Software
Figure 7.10 shows the circuit diagram for the washing machine. Integrating all of the
post­processing machines in a software­controlled environment would be a way to opti­
mise the kernels further. The AM Farm project plan describes a centralised job generation
station. By linking the part geometry and resin from the job generation system to the post­
processing system, it will become possible to customise the cleaning parameters. It might
be a good investment to have an operator conduct a study on the most effective parame­
ters for a certain part if it has been ordered in a large quantity. Contentious optimisation
of the process parameters could also be achieved over time using machine vision and
machine learning. This could ultimately eliminate the need for operator intervention and
allow the system to choose the best parameters for a given geometry and resin.
9.3.4
Safety
The study of different solvent types showed promising results for alternatives to IPA. Im­
plementing Anmasi can be the first step in improving operator safety, even in a manual
post­processing setting. Implementing the automated system eliminates the need for op­
erator interaction with uncured resin. The job of the operator can pivot to maintaining
the machines in the AM Farm. This increases the safety for the operator significantly, as
periodic maintenance of the machines will be the only time, where a human might come
in contact with resin.
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
113
==PAGE:126==
10
Conclusion
This master thesis focuses on automating the cleaning and removal of excess resin from
parts and build plates in the hazardous post­processing phase of VPP AM. Specifically,
a centrifuge and a washing machine suitable for automation were developed and tested
against manual cleaning methods.
How can the hazardous post­processing steps in VPP be automated, to minimise risk to
human health?
The proposed solutions were designed within the context of an AM Farm, assuming a
setup with at least 10 resin printers using the same resin and a minimum print time of 2
hours. Automating the hazardous phase, defined as the period until the parts are safely
positioned in a UV­curing device, allows operators to handle parts post­curing without
exposure to photopolymer resin.
Can the cleaning process be automated to ensure that parts are as clean as those cleaned
manually?
A washing machine was developed on the basis of an extensive study of existing and
available solvents and cleaning methods. It was concluded that the solvent Anmasi was
the most suitable due to its low flash­point, operator safety, price, and high saturation
levels, compared to the industry standard IPA. Anmasi has also been shown to be more
gentle to fine details on parts compared to IPA. Washing with ultrasonication and swirling
was found to be the most suitable method to clean the parts.
A MVP for the washing module has been developed using off­the­shelf ultrasonic cham­
bers in combination with custom­made components. It was found that running the washing
machine with 4­minute ultrasonication at 60 °C using Anmasi while swirling the build plate
at 20 RPM, yielded the best results. Another washing machine containing deionised water
was used to rinse the parts for 1 minute with ultrasonication to remove the contaminated
solvent.
The combination of these machines provided parts that were, on average, similar in weight
to the hand­cleaned parts using the standard cleaning procedure from DTU. Visual inspec­
tion and LOM showed how the parts cleaned with the fully automated system contained
less excess resin in blind holes on the developed benchmark part used for testing.
What measures can be implemented in the post­processing chain to ensure an econom­
ically and environmentally sustainable AM Farm?
It was found that the centrifuge could consistently remove >70% of excess resin on the
benchmark parts tested while spinning at 800 RPM for 9 minutes. Removal of the excess
resin allows for the possibility to collect and reuse this resin for further printing. The sav­
ings from reusing >70% resin, which was otherwise considered waste, are expected to
enable the centrifuge to break even in the first year of operation assuming that full­scale
production of the AM Farm has been achieved.
The proposed MVP solutions can automatically post­process prints within a 12­minute takt
time, preventing bottlenecks in the post­processing steps. The centrifuge and washing
machine developed in this thesis represent a significant step forward in the automation of
VPP post­processing, aligning with the broader goals of the AM Farm.
114
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:127==
11
Future Work
For furthering the development of the cleaning module of the AM Farm, there are certain
areas in which the efforts could be focused in order to tackle the issues which were dis­
covered during the project. The following section will discuss which these area are and
suggest which next steps could be taken to efficiently increase the potential of the AM
Farm.
11.1
Centrifuge
The centrifuge was able to remove over 70% of the resin from parts. But a pressing
question remains:
What is the ultimate limit of this centrifuge’s efficiency, and upon what variables does it
depend?
To answer this question, several areas need to be researched to increase knowledge of
the centrifugal cleaning process. A first step would be to determine the relationship be­
tween the mass of the part and the support structure holding it, for answering the question
How fast can it spin before a particular part detaches and flys off?. An additional study
determining the parameters needed in the print setup for achieving a sufficient build plate
adhesion so the part including support and raft does not detach and fly off. Different types
of resins and its strength in the green state could also be examined to determine the lim­
its for each resin, which could be done via the study presented in Section 9.1.1. If these
parameters were to be optimised the centrifuge cleaning process could in theory be used
for completely cleaning all types parts for residual resins elimination the need for solvents
and saving large amounts of resin.
The since the centrifuge was developed as a MVP solution it has several areas of im­
provement. The first would be to implement an automatic safety­lid to improve the safety
of the centrifuge. Improvements to the motor control, such as a higher degree of speed
control to enhance rotational accuracy and position control, so the collaborative robot
arm find the build plates position in the centrifuge. Additionally, sensors to monitor if build
plates are succesfully mounted should be implemented to ensure it can not spin with a
major weight offset, which results in large vibrations and ultimately catastrophic failures.
Centrifuge software should be integrated into the central manufacturing system so that it
could eventually reach light­out operation. Sensors for tracking resin collection would be
needed when the production is scaling up and larger amounts of resin will be collected.
11.2
Washing Machine
The washing machine successfully washed parts at 4 min ultrasonication, 20 RPM and 60
°C. Just like the centrifuge, it would be interesting to further optimise the washing process
parameters. To do this, the following would be the highest priority research and pursuit
areas to optimise the system. During the cleaning validation surface cracks were seen on
the parts, thus developing a solution where the cavitation intensity could be varied, would
enable entirely custom ultrasonic cleaning of parts. It could be interesting to expore how
the needed cleaning time is related to the surface area of a par, and see if the cleaning
time could be further minimised. Automation enabling features such as solvent saturation
monitoring via a hydrometer, built plate presence sensors, and a vapour­tight lid when
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
115
==PAGE:128==
the washing machine is in operation. In addition, further improving sound damping and
reducing the noise level. Connecting the washing machine control to a centralised control
system for automation between modules would be need for light­out manufacturing. But a
finishing the prototype UI for manually controlling the parameters on the washing machine
and starting a wash, would enable better interaction with the machine for more research
on its functionality and the process settings.
11.3
Future Vision of AM Farms
The future of AM farms powered by resin printing is unlocked by breakthroughs in the field,
driven by the expiration of critical patents. Once the CLIP technology patent expires, and
with rapid advancements in VPP print processes alongside innovations in recycling ther­
moset polymers, AM farms could revolutionise manufacturing to render injection moulding
obsolete. VPP as a universal solution for product manufacturing will catalyse advance­
ments across numerous scientific fields, transcending the limitations of traditional injection
moulding and machining.

Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms

A.3
Calculations
Bending stress due to imbalance
The centrifugal force Fc generated by the imbalance mass m of 200 g spinning at an
angular velocity ω is given by:
Fc = m · ω2 · r
(A.1)
First, convert the rotational speed from RPM to radians per second:
ω = 2π · RPM
60
(A.2)
For RPM = 1500:
ω = 2π · 1500
60
≈157.08 rad/s
(A.3)
The centrifugal force is calculated:
Fc = 0.2 · (157.08)2 · 0.135
(A.4)
Fc ≈665.46 N
(A.5)
The deflection δ of the shaft can be calculated using the formula for the deflection of a
cantilever beam with an end load:
δ = Fc · L3
3 · E · I
(A.6)
where:
L = length of the shaft (0.177 m)
E = Young’s modulus of the shaft material (for 304 stainless steel, E ≈193 GPa = 193 × 109 Pa)
I = second moment of area for a circular cross­section
The second moment of area I for a circular shaft is given by:
I = π · d4
64
(A.7)
where d is the diameter of the shaft (25 mm = 0.025 m).
a. Calculate the centrifugal force Fc:
Fc = 0.2 · (157.08)2 · 0.135
(A.8)
Fc ≈665.46 N
(A.9)
b. Calculate the second moment of area I:
I = π · (0.025)4
64
(A.10)
I ≈3.067 × 10−10 m4
(A.11)
140
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:153==
c. Calculate the deflection δ:
δ =
665.46 · (0.177)3
3 · 193 × 109 · 3.067 × 10−10
(A.12)
δ ≈5.57 × 10−4 m
(A.13)
δ ≈0.557 mm
(A.14)
The bending moment M at the base of the cantilever shaft due to the centrifugal force Fc
is given by:
M = Fc · L
(A.15)
The bending stress σ at the base of the shaft can be calculated using the bending stress
formula:
σ = M · c
I
(A.16)
where:
M = bending moment
c = distance from the neutral axis (for a circular shaft, c = d
2)
I = second moment of area (previously calculated as 3.067 × 10−10 m4)
Step­by­step calculation.
Bending moment M:
M = 665.46 · 0.177
(A.17)
M ≈117.78 Nm
(A.18)
Distance c:
c = d
2 = 0.025
2
(A.19)
c = 0.0125 m
(A.20)
Bending stress σ:
σ = 117.78 · 0.0125
3.067 × 10−10
(A.21)
σ ≈4.80 × 106 Pa
(A.22)
σ ≈4.80 MPa
(A.23)
The bending stress experienced at the base of the shaft due to the imbalance is approxi­
mately 4.80 MPa.

// Function to read a single digit from the serial input
2
int readSingleDigit() {
3
Serial.println("Enter test number"); // Prompt user to enter a test number
4
int number = -1; // Initialize with an invalid value
5
while (number == -1) {
6
// Check if data is available to read
7
if (Serial.available() > 0) {
8
char input = Serial.read(); // Read the input character
9
// Check if the input character is a digit (0-9)
10
if (input >= '0' && input <= '9') {
11
number = input - '0'; // Convert the character to an integer
12
}
13
// Clear any remaining characters in the serial buffer
14
while (Serial.available() > 0) {
15
Serial.read();
16
}
17
}
18
}
19
return number; // Return the valid single digit number
20
}

void loop() {
69
int digit = readSingleDigit() - 1; // Read a single digit number from the
serial input and adjust for zero-based index
70
Serial.print("You entered: ");
71
Serial.println(digit + 1);
72

delay(startdelay); // Delay before starting the motor
74
75
// Gradually increase PWM signal to the desired value
76
for (int i = 0; i <= PWM[digit]; i++) {
77
delay(acc_delay); // Delay for acceleration
78
analogWrite(pwmPin, i); // Write PWM value to the pin
79
}
80
81
delay(runtime[digit]); // Maintain the PWM signal for the specified
runtime
82
83
analogWrite(pwmPin, 0); // Stop the motor by setting PWM to 0
84
}

void RotateBuildPlate(uint16_t rotationSpeed) {
301
rotAtHome = false;
302
bool direction = true;
// True for one direction , false
for the other
303
startTime = millis();
// Track the time where rotation
started (ms after the wash started)
304
uint32_t lastPrintTime = millis();
// Track the last time the time message
was printed
305
motorRot.setAcceleration(100);
// The desired acceleration in steps
per second per second (doe 40)
306
307
float StepsRotationSpeed = rotationSpeed * 6.67;
// Converting rotation
speed from RPM to steps/s
308
309
while ((millis() - startTime) < CLEANING_TIME_MS) {
148
Development of Automated Post­Processing solutions for VPP Additive Manufacturing Farms
==PAGE:161==
310
// Check for Serial input to stop cleaning
311
312
// Set the direction and speed
313
motorRot.setMaxSpeed(direction ? StepsRotationSpeed : -StepsRotationSpeed)
;
314
315
// Move to the target position based on the direction
316
targetPosition = direction ? (300 * rotationSpeed) : 0;
317
motorRot.moveTo(targetPosition);
318
319
// Run the motor until the target position is reached
320
while (motorRot.distanceToGo() != 0 && (millis() - startTime) <
CLEANING_TIME_MS) {
321
motorRot.run();
322
323
// Log the elapsed and remaining time once every second
324
if (millis() - lastPrintTime >= 1000) {

def send_command(arduino , command , output_text):
38

39

40

F A
M
Describe the common goal and vision for the project 
 
Vision 
AM-Farm will set up the first Danish photopolymerization based Additive Manufacturing (AM) Farm 
that will cater to agile intermediate-sized production (1000-100,000 components) that can surpass 
injection molding in agility, and volumetric specific energy consumption (kJ/cm3). While injection 
molding is highly efficient at scale (>MM components), the energy consumption and tooling costs 
make it unattractive for intermediate-sized production. AM-Farm is thus the establishment of an au-
tonomous industrial AM facility with an unrivaled productivity, bespoke mass production capability 
and built on multiple cardinal sustainable innovations.  
Vat photopolymerization AM (VPP) is the method that is unsurpassed in its capability to produce 
plastic components at the highest geometrical fidelity. It has revolutionized how bespoke and minia-
turized components such as hearing aid shells are manufactured. While in many aspects, an ideal 
next generation manufacturing process, the wide industrial implementation has been deterred due 
to considerate amounts of chemical waste generated. 
AM-Farm congregates a consortium, empowered by new technological advances spun out of aca-
demia to set up the world’s first environmentally conscious facility to fully exploit the potential of 
VPP. The industrial partners aim to exploit the AM-Farm, internally as a next-generation manufac-
turing method, and as a profitable service available to other Danish manufacturers for intermediate-
sized bespoke production. 
 
Background 
Vat photopolymerisation is part of the polymer process family of Additive Manufacturing technolo-
gies. AM has created a direct linkage between the digital and physical manufacturing, thus nourish-
ing by its nature a wider trend; the digitization and the automation of the manufacturing industry. For 
this reason, the increasing adoption of AM within the manufacturing industry is pushing companies 
to research new ways of adapting their manufacturing models and optimize their manufacturing 
strategies by integrating the technologies of tomorrow into existing production and bolster their 
strategies towards a digital to physical conversion. 
 
Additive Manufacturing methods that rely on the selective photo-initiated polymerisation held in a 
resin container are known as vat photopolymerisation definition given by the ISO/ASTM 
52900:2015 naming convention. These processes rely either on the digital driven scan-line rasteris-
ing of an image by means of a laser point source or on the projection of image masks from a projec-
tion source or LCD mask, to selectively consolidate the photopolymer to the workpiece. 
 
The high component fidelity and unprecedented ability to create highly complex geometries are ob-
vious drivers for adopting vat photopolymerisation for a wide variety of manufacturing engineering 
applications, and readily available systems allow resolving geometry into 15µm voxels, while re-
search systems allow sub-micrometer resolution. There are two major impediments for a broader 
adoption of the technology for general purpose manufacturing engineering. The first impediment is 
the complexity involved in the process chain, as shown in figure 1. A 7-step process chain is in-
volved in going from CAD model to finished component, wherein the second impediment is that the 
5 steps (C-F) are manually executed post processing steps.  
==PAGE:185==
 
 
 
 
File number: 2081-00004B 
Page 3 of 18 
F A
M
 
Figure 1: the process chain involved in vat photo polymerisation 
 
The second impediment is that throughout the process chain going from feedstock to component, 
all steps but the additive manufacture are carried out by manual means, relying on the etiquette of 
the operator to supply consistency in the production. 
 
Generally, the most commonly used method for plastics production is injection moulding (IM). The 
energy consumption of the IM process for mass production has been widely studied [1]. However, 
the energy budget in material preparation (compounding) and the mould tool manufacturing are of-
ten considered cost drivers instead of efficiency metrics. This additional energy spent in the IM pro-
cess chain becomes significant, especially for intermediate volume production where the tooling 
turns to scrap soon after (< 100,000 parts). A common metric used to evaluate the energy efficiency 
is the volumetric specific energy consumption (VSEC) that is typically between 4-8 kJ/cm3 for large-
volume IM but can be much higher for intermediate volume production (20-25 kJ/cm3, as estimated 
by Krebs & Co, and Carmo). 
 
The vat photopolymerisation process eliminates the thermal cycles during material manufacturing 
(compounding) and eliminates tooling, thus improving the energy budget. Several research papers 
attempt to calculate the energy budget for specific benchmark AM components [2-4] with widely 
varying VSEC values from 10-40 kJ/cm3. Two factors that influence this variability are the nesting 
of components to improve volumetric efficiency and layer thickness changes. Optimizing the VPP 
process has the potential to make it dramatically more energy efficient than IM while being more 
cost-effective for intermediate volume production. 
 
[1] Kent, R. (2008). Energy management in plastics processing—framework for measurement, as-
sessment and prediction. Plastics, Rubber and Composites, 37(2-4), 96-104. 
[2] Hopkins, N., Jiang, L., & Brooks, H. (2021). Energy consumption of common desktop additive 
manufacturing technologies. Cleaner Engineering and Technology, 2, 100068. 
[3] Gutierrez-Osorio, A. H., Ruiz-Huerta, L., Caballero-Ruiz, A., Siller, H. R., & Borja, V. (2019). En-
ergy consumption analysis for additive manufacturing processes. The International Journal of Ad-
vanced Manufacturing Technology, 105(1), 1735-1743.

Job generation
B) Additive 
Manufacture
C) Chemical
Rinsing
D) Drying
E) Post Curing
E) Support

F) Tumble polishing

F A

[4] Yoon, H. S., et. Al. (2014). A comparison of energy consumption in bulk forming, subtractive, and 
additive processes: Review and case study. International Journal of Precision Engineering and Man-
ufacturing- Green Technology, 1(3), 261-279. 
 
Objectives and Success Criteria 
The overall goal of the AM Farm project is to establish a manufacturing-farm facility. An AM farm is 
the manifestation of a seamlessly integrated allotment of a quantity of smaller AM systems that in a 
collaborative arrangement stack up their manufacturing capacity to that of a high-throughput large 
scale manufacturing facility. The efficacy comes by a dynamic expansion potential, the bulk of 
smaller AM systems instil resilience in the facility by the capacity to take individual AM systems out 
and offline for repair and maintenance with negligible adverse impact to the farm in its entirety. AM 
systems can be clustered into kernels, or “fields” within the farm, arranged by e.g. what process 
material type and properties such as e.g. color is currently fitted within the kernel. Rolling hardware 
upgrades can be executed, replacing individual AM systems to latest state-of-art, again clustering 
said systems into kernels by hardware model and hardware specifications. 
The farm installment of production systems transcends the reasoning that goes into conventional 
manufacturing facility operations, offering in agility, expandability and robustness what cannot be 
achieved when operating a single piece of high-volume manufacturing equipment. More specifically 
the project objectives are: 
 
• 
The establishment of an automated and energy efficient Danish AM farm which is competi-
tive against injection moulding for small-mid size production (1000 – 100000 components), 
which in turn allows 
o 
to take in orders for short batches that currently are too modest to be suitable for in-
jection molding, thus dramatically increasing business aptitude;  
o 
to establish and hone the competitive edge by increasing production efficiency and 
capacity such that also larger production runs can be executed;  
o 
Success: Reach a Volumetric Specific Energy consumption of 5 kJ/cm3 as meas-
ured by the energy utilized and number of parts produced by the farm 
 
• 
Positioning of Krebs & Co and Carmo as first movers enabling them to self-disrupt the in-
dustry they are players within (IM) by daring to be first-movers and adopt VPP based addi-
tive manufacturing as their next-gen manufacturing technology. 
Success: 20 customers with AM orders > 1000 components delivered from the 
Farm capturing customers from a business perspective that was not feasible by IM  
• 
To instil environmental consciousness into a production method that this far is instantiated 
in niches where austerity has been the argument to disregard this; 
o 
to propel the industrial and academic partners to be defining for the sustainability 
agenda within AM and being a global beacon for change; 
o 
to focus of workplace safety for employees handling photopolymers 
o 
Success: Reduce the interaction by operators with photopolymers by 60 %  
 
• 
To establish automation-based technology modules for part handling and post-processing

Value creation 
The value chain of injection moulding of plastic products is defined as traditional manufacturing in 
figure 2. Industrial participation from Krebs & co and Carmo covers product and production engi-
neering, production of plastic components, storage of components to be supplied to customers 
within the plastic market ecosystem. Green boxes of figure 2 represent parts of the value chain that 
the companies are currently operating in. The collaboration with DTU enable a paradigm shift in the 
production of small and intermediate batch volume of complex plastic components. Moving cus-
tomer demand at the beginning of the AM-Farm value chain will be empowered by the successful 
implantation of the AM facilities ultimately addressing high efficiency on demand production. Value 
chain innovation is supported by the introduction of the AM-Farm academia and industrial technol-
ogy program development. Agility and flexibility inherent of the proposed approach allow for produc-
tion efficiency and sustainable approach to small and intermediate production. The access to excel-
lence through DTU is the key enabling factor for the realisation of this new technological approach 
when addressing the possibility to create a shared R&D innovation centres digitally linked between 
the private and public actors. 
 
 
Figure2: Value creation from FARM compared to traditional manufacturing 
 
The AM-Farm has the ability to support clients who are not clear whether their products will be true 
high volume production setups or whether it will stay in the lower volume segments. Specifically for 
many start-up companies and new innovations, it is advantageous to be able to operate with an ag-
ile setup during ramp up to allow for continued developments in the product as feedback from the 
market is received after the initial launch. Furthermore the potential for not having to stock spare

Given a successful completion of the project, a stand-alone company will be established to deliver 
advisory services related to “how to get started with design for AM” and “Advanced AM-Farm capa-
bilities”. Carmo and Krebs & Co will promote their AM-Farm services together on a digital platform 
along with participation in trade fairs and industrial seminars. It is believed that a substantial part of 
the business can be driven on a digital platform, given the nature of the service. Part of the future 
market share for this will also come from a transition of tooling investments that can be pooled into 
manufacturing via the AM-Farm. The part costs will go up when manufacturing via VPP, but this will 
be outweighed by the savings made in tooling and the reduced environmental impact. 
 
 
Exit strategy 
 
KREBS & CO AND CARMO 
The two industrial partners within the consortium are collaborating manufacturers with expertise in 
medical device manufacturing and employ various plastic processing methods such as injection 
moulding in their production. The intent is to self-disrupt their own industry by daring to be first-mov-
ers and adopt VPP based additive manufacturing as their next-gen manufacturing technology. 
 
Both companies have a track record for applying VPP based methods for prototyping and for short-
series production but not on a systematic level and not at a currently scalable adoption level. Fore-
casting current component sales levels from their joint client base, the partnering companies expect 
that an increase in revenue of no less than 45M dkk achievable over  5-year period after project 
completion. As a consequence of this it is expected that approximately 12 new jobs in their compa-
nies adjointly will be created.,  
 
During the formulation of this project proposal, it became evermore clear that the true value for 
Krebs & Co and Carmo does not reside in a revenue boost but in the capability to adopt new pro-
duction paradigms, offer new services and solutions to their clients. There is a vertical growth po-
tential herein as well as a potential to leapfrog their competition by an order of magnitude by re-
thinking the utilization of plastic components as relates to their design, their production and their 
consumption. The technological platform that will be created across industry and academia will en-
sure state of the art technology, material and process based development.  
 
Initially the strategy is to go for clients needing complex components and having high requirements. 
The strategy will most likely develop in 3 distinct phases.  
 
Phase 1 – Selectively adoption of the technology with the existing client base by offering the attrac-
tive AM Farm solution to the clients, where it makes business sense.  
 
Phase 2 – Identify most promising customer segments and attract new clients from top 3 segments 
to further boost volume and showcase viability, capabilities and scalability. 
 
Phase 3 – Establishing standalone business unit(s) offering services to AM Farm production, advi-
sory and expansion. Further to the described services, the strategy also include the opportunity to 
sell licenses for hardware solution technologies.  
 
==PAGE:189==
 
 
 
 
File number: 2081-00004B 
Page 7 of 18 
F A
M
Specifically for the hardware solutions, it is envisioned that this will be spun off in to separate busi-
ness entities, due to the difference in business model and go to market strategy.  
 
In phase 3 the standalone business unit(s) will explore opportunities to include investors and addi-
tional industrial partners into the consortium to ensure we will have access to additional funding and 
capabilities. Both elements are paramount to ensure a rapid expansion and roll-out of the solutions 
and services. 
 
Throughout the duration of the project, a constant focus is on knowledge transfer from the aca-
demic partner to the industrial partners. Focus will be given to up-qualifying their staff to work with a 
next-gen production facility such as the one envisioned within this project. This will be substantiated 
through joint workshops and educational activities for the employees in the companies, facilitated 
by the academic partner. 
 
The AM group at DTU Civil and Mechanical Engineering has for a decade pushed VPP technology 
capability beyond state-of-art. Staining this effort has been a lack of industrial adoption of VPP 
based additive manufacturing for general manufacturing purposes due to the aforementioned barri-
ers that the Danish manufacturing industry face. By joining the consortium DTU has the opportunity 
to work towards removing existing implementation barrier of VPP in a broader context. The environ-
mental issues that inherently follows with a manufacturing process in which highly reactive materi-
als are processed. The AM-Farm project will furthermore serve as a technology demonstrator 
wherein knowledge that has been built over the past decade can be set free and roam wild. Thus, 
AM-Farm is a proactive initiative that embrace the concept of an industry/academia alliance for the 
wider adoption of VPP for tomorrows demanding plastic component production that aspire to bring 
VPP technology to the Danish industry for the decade to come. 
 
Master plan for the whole project 
 
There is an unrealized potential in establishing an additive manufacturing facility that caters to man-
ufacturing small to mid batch size (1000-100,000) production of mass-customizable complex-
shaped components. An AM farm is an allotment of a quantity of smaller AM systems that are 
seamlessly integrated into one large manufacturing facility. The efficacy comes by a dynamic ex-
pansion potential, coupled with the resilience in the facility by the capacity to take individual AM 
systems out offline for repair and maintenance with negligible adverse impact to the farm in its en-
tirety. AM systems can be clustered into kernels, or “fields” within the farm, arranged by e.g. what 
process material type and color is currently fitted. Rolling hardware upgrades can be executed, re-
placing individual AM systems to latest state-of-art, again clustering said systems into kernels by 
hardware model. The farm instalment of production systems transcends the reasoning that goes 
into conventional manufacturing facility operations, offering in agility, expandability and robustness 
what cannot be achieved when operating a single piece of high-volume manufacturing equipment. 
 
Vat photopolymerisation is the AM method that by far produces components of the highest geomet-
rical fidelity and thus, in many cases directly be a rivalling manufacturing method for especially 
smaller, highly complex, injection moulded components. There is but one caveat that above all ne-
gates the utility and the practical implementation of an AM farm based on vat photopolymerization. 
The farm model is contested by the amount of manual labour involved in part finishing.  
Hence, there is a fundamental need to rethink how the process is implemented through automation, 
for the farm model is to be realized by means of vat photopolymerization. The main challenge is in 
==PAGE:190==
 
 
 
 
File number: 2081-00004B 
Page 8 of 18 
F A
M
that the agility of the farm model is the capability of running small production batches of highly dif-
fering components on a to-order basis which encumbers the automation of the process further 
given that conventional automation tasks typically favour simple, repetitive, and identical tasks. 
The AM-Farm project will bridge the benefits of agility and resilience to expedite short-orderings of 
high-end components in small and intermediate production allotment for the benefit of the Danish 
manufacturing sector through new methods for automation and part handling as seen in figure 3. 
This will concurrently solve the automation challenge that envelop, and as to date, has limited the 
wider adoption of vat photopolymerisation at scale, as well as introducing a new and energy effi-
cient means of producing high quality plastic components. 
 
Krebs & Co and Carmo will demonstrate to themselves and to the Danish manufacturing industry, 
how a next-gen production facility can be a solution to limit energy consumption while disrupting 
their competitors in the miniaturization and precision injection moulding industry. The AM-farm facil-
ity will bring an agility to the partner companies that will vastly outperform their competitors in the 
conventional injection moulding segment, allowing for the partners to offer small production batches 
of highly intricate components with no tooling cost, no setup cost and at a lower energy consump-
tion as compared to Injection Moulding especially for small-mid size batch production. 
The AM farm will be established in a sequential order from VPP infrastructure and through the pro-
cess chain comprised of all the necessary post-processing steps, and their accompanying hardware 
for chemical washing, post curing and finishing. As the consortium builds up the VPP farm demon-
strator facility, the academic partner will imbue the process chain with automation implementation to 
ensure that the production is scalable at a minimum of manual labour, offering the Danish manufac-
turing industry with a lighthouse demonstrator.

AM FARM Modules 
For many of the automation solutions needed for the factory to be a viable and green alternative to 
a conventional plastic processing facility, bespoke hardware should to be developed and, in some 
cases, industrial systems can be exploited through modifications. As such, setting up operations will 
not be a standard solution as could otherwise be adopted from industry niches that have already 
made the transition to VPP based production. Given this, in the latter part of the project, focus will 
be on running the facility as a demonstrator and simultaneously a profitable business while solving 
technical issues that relates to the bespoke infrastructure that the facility is built upon. These innno-
vations are referred to in this document as “FARM modules” with a preference towards robustness 
in manufacturing layered upon with automation. Figure 4 below shows the various modules that 
need to be developed in two stages. The first stage represents a minimum viable FARM, which is 
can accept orders from customers, but with a current limitation of upto 1000 components due to the 
manual post-processing steps. Following the minimum viable FARM, commercialization activities 
begin. Stage-2 takes a robust FARM and converts it into an automated facility. The hierarchy of the 
FARM modules represents the order in which they will be implemented such that the FARM can 
generate business as soon as possible. 
 
Benchmarking of AM FARM Modules: Each module requires to meet some specifications which can 
be geometry dependent. Hence it is critical for the robustness of the FARM to test, optimize and 
benchmark each module. Two demonstrators (DD1 and DD2) are chosen which are representative 
parts with multiple features of interest. DD1 is a coarse demonstrator where the smallest feature is 
0.5 mm, while DD2 is a fine demonstrator where the smallest feature of interest is 50 um. DD3 is a 
support structure benchmark with various overhanging features ranging from 100 um to 50 mm 
overhanging features including flat and curved surfaces.

STAGE 1 - Minimum Viable Farm 
 
VPP SYSTEM SELECTION AND PREPARATION OF VPP SYSTEM FOR AUTOMATION 
 
The industrial partners will install a matrix of VPP systems in a joint facility. This will be in the form 
of an industrial shelving system to support a matrix of VPP machines, which can dynamically be ex-
panded horizontally and vertically. The instalment will be set up such that, at a later stage, a ware-
house-style robot rail system can be used to automate the VPP systems with respect to removing 
manufactured components while on their build platforms and inserting new platforms into the VPP 
machines. It is important that the VPP system of choice offers the ability to use third party materials, 
which is not the norm for most VPP systems providers. Selecting such a system will be the first task 
(MS1,MS2). 
The first research and development task will relate to the digital infrastructure that tethers each of 
these machines to a print management and queueing system. Such a system is not readily availa-
ble as an industry solution with a low-level machine access. A system level access required to en-
sure that the VPP machines can be controlled by a custom job generator and be allowed to process 
third party materials. The academic partner will play a key role in establishing this. The key imple-
ments allow for: 
 
• Direct hardware access on a select AM system on which the AM Farm will be based, such that a 
universal job-queueing solution can be implemented. This involves the reverse engineering of the 
job-file format for the select AM system and an implementation of a centralized job generation and 
queueing software solution. A Beta version of such an implementation was recently demonstrated 
at DTU (MS3, MS4). 
 
• Processing of third-party resins. Most AM systems currently on the market offers a narrow selec-
tion of genuine feedstock that can be introduced to the machines and the use of third-party feed-
stock are restricted by not offering the operator to alter processing parameters. For the AM-Farm to 
be of interest for clients of a component manufacturer such as Carmo and Krebs & Co over conven-
tional manufacturing processes, the farm must allow for processing of the largest possible range of 
materials. This is partly solved through the aforementioned reverse engineering of the job-genera-
tion system which will allow the industrial partners to freely access machine parameters. The effec-
tive and successful introduction of a third party feedstock is governed by the identification of the ap-
propriate processing parameter window. A methodology with an automated testing regime will be 
developed to most effectively calibrate a new material for processing by the AM farm. 
 
• The introduction of optimized support structures and support structure interfaces. Support struc-
tures provide structural stability to otherwise free-floating parts of the 3D printed component that 
would collapse during manufacturing. These are conventionally simple scaffolding pillars that are 
broken off by hand during the post processing of the component. A novel concept has been demon-
strated at DTU by which support can be generated such that the support structures leave a mini-
mum of scarring on the interface to the component and does not require human labour to remove. 
This innovation exploits the introduction of a gel-layer, formed by lowering the UV exposure to the 
photopolymer resin in the interface against the part that subsequently can be washed off, hereby 
vastly simplifying the support removal step in the process chain and allowing for fully automated 
support removal. 
==PAGE:193==
 
 
 
 
File number: 2081-00004B 
Page 11 of 18 
F A
M
 
•  Preparation for automation of build plate removal and insertion into the VPP system by engineer-
ing a unified connector interface. This interface will allow for the efficient transfer of a build plate 
from the VPP system to the automation solution that handles build plate insertion, removal and 
transfer downstream throughout the process chain. This interface is critical as the build plate needs 
to be docked with micrometer accuracy to ensure that the first 3D printed layer adhere to the build 
platform. If not handled correctly, build failure occurs. This interface is a critical engineering task on 
the design of an appropriate kinematic coupling solution, (MS5, MS6). 
 
Ultimately, for the success of stage 1, the most appropriate VPP system must initially be selected. A 
selection that determines whether stage 1 will succeed. As most VPP systems are of a highly pro-
prietary nature that does not allow for easy hardware control and less the introduction of third party 
materials, a wrong choice of system can be detrimental for the project. DTU Civil and Mechanical 
Engineering has a decade of a priori knowledge on the reverse engineering and the substitution of 
control systems in VPP based AM infrastructure. The risk of selecting a wrong platform to base AM 
farm on, can ultimately be remedies by replacing the VPP system controller with hardware already 
developed at DTU and adapted to fit the VPP system. The consortium has identified one system 
that has potential. It is a VPP system at a competitive investment cost, with a 4K image mask-
based UV light engine and bottom exposure. The system is ethernet capable and the encrypted job 
file structure has already been reverse engineered by the AM group at DTU. Thus, a custom job 
generator can be developed to accommodate the above requirements and low-level machine in-
structions can be injected via ethernet. Stage 1, can be completed by Month 15 of the project. 
 
STAGE 2 - Progress towards fully automated FARM 
 
Stage 2 in the AM-farm project is where AM-farm fully transcends to a next-gen production facility 
and is what allows for the energy efficient AM-Farm to compete on throughput as compared to In-
jection Moulding. Up to stage 2, the physical demonstrator setup of AM-Farm resembles the current 
VPP production facilities that operates in e.g. the hearing aid niche. In stage 1, the foundation for a 
fully automated production was laid. The vertical stage on each VPP machine is equipped with a 
kinematic coupling that will be universal to the entire production line, and which allows for a robot 
effector to interface with the build platforms. The VPP machine matrix will be equipped with an in-
ventory-style robot to automate build platform insertion and removal. Platforms taken from the ma-
trix will be docked with a receptacle onto a conveyor that takes the build platform to the washing 
station. While on the platforms, handled through the kinematic coupling, the build platform under-
goes a washing cycle computed from the component geometry on the platform to ensure autono-
mous and reliable component washing while minimizing energy consumption and solvent consump-
tion. Once the build plate has been removed, a sensor system will fill the Vat with more photopoly-
mer. 
 
The inherent issue with rinsing of components to remove uncured photopolymer, is that the process 
produces the mixed-chemistry waste stream that currently only can be separated by running the 
waste stream through a refinery still which is a high energy consumption means of recovering the 
solvent. Therefore it is the norm that the waste material disposed of by incineration, that arguably 
expends more energy. DTU has invented a solvent recovery system under Danish Patent No. PA 
2019 70548, that is a low-energy method to separate resin from solvent. The solvent can be reused 
in a closed washing cycle and the separated resin is cured and can be disposed of as clean plastic 
waste. The academic partner is responsible for leading the engineering of a closed-cycle washing 
machine with built in solvent recovery joined by the industrial partners for scale-up of the solution 
==PAGE:194==
 
 
 
 
File number: 2081-00004B 
Page 12 of 18 
F A
M
and subsequent industrial implementation. This will be set up along with the solutions for post cur-
ing, support removal and polishing, (MS7). 
 
Following this, the build platform is moved down the line to a drying station to rapidly remove any 
remaining volatiles before the platform meets the automated post curing cell. This cell will be up-
graded with an UV light modulation engine such that the dose of UV irradiation during post curing 
can be tailored to the component by means of a dynamic UV source, reducing energy consumption 
of this post processing step as well. This dynamic post curing method is the third innovation which 
is currently being processed as an invention at DTU with the intent to pursue a patent, to be trans-
ferred to the industry partners during project execution, (MS8). 
 
Ultimately, the components are autonomously released from their gel-support and introduced, from 
operators in an initial semi-automatic procedure, into a tumble polisher to bring up the component 
surface to the desired gloss finish. Once implemented, AM-Farm will be the world’s first VPP pro-
duction facility that can operate autonomously and produce industrial grade miniaturized plastic 
components with a vastly lower energy consumption than current injection moulders, presenting it-
self close to the environmental ideal, (MS9,MS11). 
 
Exploitation of the FARM modules 
The advantage of the FARM automation in modules is that we expect to find and implement solu-
tions that improve work-conditions (operator interaction with uncured photopolymer) (MS10). Fur-
ther, using AM instead of IM provides a unique traceability across the process chain for each com-
ponent. Vision systems (camera) will be implemented coupled with QR codes on build plates to en-
sure end-to-end traceability and eliminate defective parts out of the production through regular qual-
ity checks (MS12). 
 
The consortium of partners made a conscious choice to NOT pursue end-to-end automation with 
complete software integration of the FARM (for e.g. a Manufacturing Execution System). End-to-
end automation of a lights-out FARM is not necessarily a scientific challenge, but rather comes from 
the synergy of combining the various FARM modules developed together. The cost differential to 
implementing a lights-out facility vs a highly-automated but supervised facility makes the latter op-
tion more commercially viable and more agile during the development phase. Further, it is neces-
sary to automate each step of the process chain individually before developing a lights-out facility. 
Hence the project focuses on improving the minimum viable FARM to a highly autonomous and su-
pervised FARM. 
 
A framework for IPR transfer from DTU to the industrial partners has been negotiated as a part of the 
collaboration agreement. AM-Farm will serve as the first industry-scale implementation of this solution 
and the invention will be offered to the industrial partners on lucrative conditions as first adopters and 
license holders. 
 
The AM-Farm offers the environmentally conscious solution that will allow for a successful adoption 
of VPP in a broader context than in the already established niches, as a next gen production 
method through a two pringed approach. The first prong remedies the environmental issues that 
hinder wide adoption whilst the second prong deals with the labour cost barriers originating from 
lack of automation that penalize VPP and the additive manufacturing industry in general.  
 
Value creation includes but is not limited to identification of early adopters or case-customers, that 
dare to embark on a journey towards a new production paradigm for their components. It includes 
identifying and exploiting revenue streams that can be online part ordering systems, live production 
==PAGE:195==
 
 
 
 
File number: 2081-00004B 
Page 13 of 18 
F A
M
metrics and update feeds to customers, exploring new industry segments, upselling to existing and 
insourcing VPP large volume productions, MS13, MS14. .

components. A statistically significant quality analysis is performed by optical mi-
croscopy of the geometrical features of 100 components out of 10000 components.

tracked for anomalies. Each build plate will have DD1, DD2 parts on it which are 
also measured by optical microscopy and benchmarked.
